Gevia Kesef
גביע כסף
Trans. by Basil Herring, New York, Ktav, 1982
https://www.nli.org.il/he/books/NNL_ALEPH000885949/NLI

Gevia Kesef

Preface

<sup class="footnote-marker">*</sup><i class="footnote">*Parenthetical insertions in the body of the translated text have been inserted by the translator to facilitate a clearer understanding of Kaspi’s text. Scriptural verses are translated in accordance with the 1917 JPS translation. Hence the tetragrammaton is rendered as “Lord”; <i>’Elokim</i> as “God.” <b>Annotation </b><br>In the notes that follow, references to the chapters in Part I of this book are given in arabic numbers (e.g., Chapter 1) and references to chapters of the <i>Gevia‘ Kesef</i> are given in roman numbers (e.g., chap. I).</i>Joseph Ibn Kaspi said: After giving thanks to God, who bestowed wisdom on our teacher Moses, the light of God and the light of Israel, in whose light we see light,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">1. These terms refer to Moses Maimonides, not Moses the lawgiver, for these honorifics are used by Kaspi elsewhere to describe Maimonides. See, for example, “our teacher Moses” (MK II 294; AS I 68); Maimonides as the source of illumination is mentioned on AK I 46, 51, 111.</i> I will begin by saying that the intention of this work is to write whatever I can regarding the secrets of the Torah that I did not see fit to write in the <i>Maṣref La-Kesef, Ṭirat Kesef, Mizraḳ Kesef</i>, and <i>Menorat Kesef</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">2. In the chapter introducing the <i>Gevia‘ Kesef</i>, the relation of these works to the <i>Gevia‘ Kesef</i> has been discussed.</i> I have called this book <i>Yoreh De‘ah</i> (“The Guide to Knowledge”),<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">3. In all the references to the <i>Gevia‘ Kesef</i> in Kaspi’s writings, this name is not found. One reason for mentioning this name in this context might be the desire to indicate the importance of this work as a source of knowledge. In the Munich MS of the <i>Ḳevuṣat Kesef</i>, another name, <i>’Oṣar ’Adonai</i>, is mentioned.</i> similar to the name <i>Moreh Ha-Torah</i> (“The Guide to the Torah”), for that is its meaning. Its agnomen reflecting my own name is <i>Gevia‘ Kesef</i> (“The Silver Goblet”), “it is that in which my lord drinketh.”<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">4. Gen. 44:2, 5.</i> We can begin with what is intended, with the help of the Lord.





Chapter 1

In addition to what has been written in the <i>Menorat Kesef</i> and <i>Mizraḳ Kesef</i>, (one should) know that it is correct to say of God that He is the first cause, in a sense that is compound as well as the (simple) sense of His essence.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">5. Below in n. 14 the sense in which God is “compound” is explained.</i> This is certainly true of the world of Separate Intelligences of which the Lord, may He be blessed, is part.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">6. Kaspi here refers to the existence of universe as three successive worlds, as postulated by the Aristotelian school: the world of Separate Intelligences; the world of Heavenly Spheres below that; the sublunar world of elements. This hierarchy, accepted by Ibn Ezra and Maimonides, is explained by Kaspi in detail in the <i>Menorat Kesef</i> (AS II 79 ff.). In the present passage God is described as being the highest part of the world of Separate Intelligences, and therefore “part” of the universe. Cf. above p. 28, n. 188.</i> The reason is that the world of Separate Intelligences, or if you will, the universe of the Intellect, or the universe of the Intelligences, consists of Him, blessed be He, His court and His attendants, as our Sages said. This category is divided, according to our conception, into three components, when described in terms of cause and effect,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">7. Each Intelligence is the cause of the one Intelligence below it, and it is in this sense only that they can be numbered separately. This way to distinguish them follows Maimonides, <i>Ma’amar Ha-Yiḥud</i>, ed. M. Steinschneider (Berlin, 1846), p. 5.</i> as Abu-Nasr (Al-Farabi) said (when he described them as) “the Primary One, the Secondary Ones, and the Active Intellect.”<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">8. Al-Farabi, <i>Al-Madinah Al-Fadilah</i> (Beirut, 1959), p. 23, cited in M. Fakhry, <i>A History of Islamic Philosophy</i> (New York: Columbia University Press, 1970), pp. 136 ff., refers to God as “the First.” Here “Primary One” refers to God, “Secondary Ones” refers to the Separate Intelligences as a group (<i>shniyim</i>), with the Active Intellect as the lowest of the Intelligences. Together they constitute the uppermost of the three worlds. On Kaspi’s perception of that world, see A. Altmann, “The Ladder of Ascension,” in <i>Studies in Mysticism and Religion Presented to Gershom Scholem</i> (Jerusalem: Magnes Press, 1967), p. 23.</i> You should know that it was from this that in ancient times there first emerged a belief in the Father, the Son, and the Spirit. Only later was that belief transformed into something else.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">9. This is an oblique reference to Christianity. Later in chapter V Kaspi is more explicit. Kaspi’s point here is that the Christian doctrine of the trinity has Jewish roots, a point that is discussed and approved by H. Wolfson, <i>The Philosophy of the Church Fathers</i> (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 1964), pp. 141–167, without any reference to Kaspi.</i> Indeed, the Lord, blessed be He, is the Primary One, and is the Father; the Intelligences<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">10. Literally “the Secondary Ones.”</i> are known as son or sons, as it says “sons of God,”<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">11. Gen. 6:2.</i> “sons of might,”<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">12. Ps. 29:1.</i> which is the main meaning of those verses. Certainly God is called “Father” throughout the Torah and the Bible, for He is the primary bestower of life for all. The Active Intellect is referred to as “the Spirit of God,” “the Holy Spirit,” or “the Spirit of the Lord.” All this is evident.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">13. For further discussion of the structure of the uppermost world, see M. Friedlander, <i>Essays on the Writings of Abraham Ibn Ezra</i> (London, 1877), p. 13.</i>
Accordingly, the world of Separate Intelligences is in one respect one, and in another respect many. If you will you may say (the Separate Intelligences) are many and they are one. Alternatively, | the world of Separate Intelligences as a whole is three, and is one. It was in this manner that the convention of the trinity arose. Even the Primary One (God) Himself could be described in this manner, being one unto Himself and also the threefold cause of the Intelligences and the Active Intellect.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">14. In the <i>‘Ammudei Kesef</i> (AM 70), Kaspi attributes this idea that God is the threefold cause of the universe to Al-Batalyawsi’s <i>Book of Intellectual Circles</i>. Such an idea is found in that work, in D. Kaufmann, ed., <i>Die Spuren Al-Batlajusis in der Jüdischen Religionsphilosophie</i> (Berlin, 1880), pp. 37 ff. (according to the pagination at the back). In <i>Menorat Kesef</i> (AS II 83) Kaspi himself explains that God is perceived in a threefold manner, in that not only is He the intellect and the intellectually cognizing subject as well as the intellectually cognized object (cf. <i>Guide</i> I:68), but He is also the efficient, formal and final cause of the universe (see <i>Guide</i> I:69).</i> Accordingly it could also be said of Him that He is one and He is three, even while He is absolutely one, while everything else is many, for they are compounded of and perfected by cause and effect, which is not so with God, for He has no cause. This is according to our Torah. For this reason the Men of the Great Assembly said, “They import holiness to you three times,”<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">15. This follows the Sephardic version of the <i>kedushah</i> prayer.</i> in referring to the statement of Isaiah, “Holy, holy, holy is the Lord of hosts …”<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">16. Isa. 6:3. Kimḥi, in his commentary to this verse, gives a more conventional reading, finding reference to the three worlds that praise God. The Aramaic Targum Onkelos understands the reference as to the heavens, the earth, and the infinity of time. Kaspi goes beyond them to understand the reference as being to the threefold aspect of God Himself, or that of the world of Separate Intelligences. For other, similar interpretations of this verse, most notably that of Crescas, see H. Wolfson, <i>Crescas’ Critique of Aristotle</i> (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 1929), p. 459, n. 92, where Crescas is interpreted as referring to a kabbalistic system of <i>sefirot</i>.</i> where the threefold repetition refers to the world of Separate Intelligences in general, and the Lord in particular.

Chapter 2

In the <i>Mizraḳ Kesef</i> I explained the changes that occur (regarding the names of God) in the Account of Creation, where sometimes He is called God, sometimes the Lord God, and sometimes the Lord.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">1. <i>Mizraḳ Kesef</i> is not extant. The question of the divine names in the story of Creation was raised in the nonextant <i>Kesef Sigim</i>, as is clear from <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i> (MK II 15). In the <i>Menorat Kesef</i> (AS II 88–91) Kaspi discusses this subject with reference to the opening chapters of Genesis, and considers his discussion there as complementary to that in <i>Mizraḳ Kesef</i>. From that discussion it appears that <i>’Elokim</i> was used by Moses in the first chapter of Genesis because it was a term universally recognized as referring to the maker of the universe. Of course the pagans thought the reference to be to the world of Heavenly Spheres, and Moses knew that he could not alter that perception immediately. For this reason the following references were to “tetragrammaton <i>’Elokim</i>,” i.e., the uppermost world of Separate Intelligences. Finally, the tetragrammaton alone occurs, intending the Lord Himself. In this way Moses hoped to bring the world to the recognition of the one true God.</i> I will now complete this with respect to changes occurring in the Torah after the order of Creation, beginning with “These are the generations of Noah. Noah was in his generations a man righteous and wholehearted; Noah walked with God.”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">2. Gen. 6:9.</i> Notice that, similar to Job,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">3. The <i>Guide</i> III:22 had noted that Job 1:1 describes Job as “wholehearted and upright, feared God and shunned evil” without any reference to Job’s wisdom.</i> it says that Noah was righteous, not that he was wise, for the intent is to refer to the whole narrative including Noah and his generations as far as Abraham. And even though the Lord spoke with him so that he was a prophet (and therefore must have been wise), it is as Maimonides explained—that “prophecy” and “prophet” are equivocal.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">4. <i>Guide</i> II:35, in reference to Moses’ prophecy as compared to that of other prophets.</i> Thus of Noah it is said, “Noah walked with God,” not “… with the Lord” using the ineffable name. This is like “and Enoch walked with God,”<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">5. Gen. 5:22.</i> which is written of Enoch. The use of the <i>hitpa‘el</i> is known from the study of logic as an action still in progress and incomplete.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">6. This point is discussed somewhat more by Kaspi in <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i> (MK II 302), where the <i>hitpa‘el</i> is described as referring to “an action still in the middle.” There Kaspi refers to the <i>Physics</i> of Aristotle as his source. In the <i>Physics</i>, bk. 3, 201a, Aristotle defines motion as “the fulfillment of what exists potentially, insofar as it exists potentially.” Thus Noah was potentially close to God.</i> It is thus with every usage of the <i>hitpa‘el</i> stem in Hebrew. Reliable evidence for this is “there you shall sell yourselves … for bondmen and bondwomen …,”<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">7. Deut. 28:68, <i>“Ve-hitmakartem …”</i></i> which must be in the progressive aspect with the transactions uncompleted. Proof of this is provided by “and no man shall buy you.”<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">8. At the end of the same verse.</i> Noah’s greatest perception was his recognition of the heavenly influences, similar to his contemporaries, who also did not realize that there was an existent beyond the Heavenly Spheres.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">9. Elsewhere Kaspi says that this knowledge of the uppermost world of Separate Intelligences was first revealed by Abraham.</i> For this reason he attributed most matters, and certainly the flood with the action of the wind that caused the waters to subside, as well as the other details of the story, to the causative action of the Heavenly Spheres, | which are what is intended in the name of “God” (<i>’Elokim</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">10. Accordingly, <i>’Elokim</i> is a generic name for the two upper worlds, which are the Separate Intelligences and the Heavenly Spheres, respectively. “The Lord,” as explained earlier, is part of the former.</i> Notice especially that at the outset “God” is mentioned,”<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">11. In v. 1.</i> then there is a change to the name “Lord” when it is written, “And the Lord said unto Noah: come thou and all thy house into the ark.”<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">12. Gen. 7:1.</i> This was (written) to inform us, the recipients of the Torah, that the Lord was the first cause (of those events), and that when we cling to Him we are protected from any evils caused by the constellations or the signs of heaven.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">13. These latter terms are translated in accordance with the sense of J. Klatzkin, <i>’Oṣar Ha-Munaḥim Ha-Philosophim</i> (Berlin, 1928), vol. II, p. 136. This doctrine of heavenly influences was developed most notably in Ibn Ezra’s theory of providence, as found in his commentary to Exod. 3:15. See D. Rosin, “Die Religionsphilosophie Abraham Ibn Ezras,” <i>MGWJ</i>, vol. XLII, p. 160.</i> Similarly it says, “Thus did Noah according to all that the Lord commanded him.” It was, however, very important that regarding Noah’s sacrifice to God the Torah not mention the name “God” (to whom Noah sacrificed), but only “Lord,” which is the ineffable and unique name, for the error of contemporary nations was that they sacrificed to the heavenly host. In the Torah Moses intended above all to remove that belief from us, and to have us believe in the world of the Separate Intelligences that exist in addition to the Heavenly Spheres. Moses did the same regarding (his description of) of the sacrifices of Cain and Abel.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">14. Gen. 4:3–4.</i> As for the “dispersed generation,”<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">15. Gen. 10:35, “for in his days was the earth divided (<i>niflegah ha-’areṣ</i>.)”</i> who believed exclusively in the Heavenly Spheres, it is clearly fitting to refer to Him who intervened with them as “the Lord” in His glory.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">16. Gen. 11:5.</i>

Chapter 3

When the writer of the Torah began with the stories of Abraham he wrote, “Now the Lord said to Abram: Get thee out …”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">1. Gen. 12:1.</i> for Abraham was the first one to discover a belief affirming the existence of the world of Separate Intelligences. This opinion caused him to leave his land,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">2. In <i>Ṭirat Kesef</i> (MK I 72) Abraham’s father, Terah, is described as taking the family from Ur on account of the intense hatred that the beliefs of Abraham had engendered in their contemporaries.</i> doing what he did, for which the Lord acted to save him on various occasions.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">3. In the <i>Menorat Kesef</i> (AS II 85) Abraham is reported to have written many books proving the existence of God beyond the world of Heavenly Spheres. These books were later stolen by the Greeks and Arabs; consequently the proofs of God’s existence as found in Aristotle were initially proposed by Abraham. Cf. above p. 25.</i> Consequently in the stories of Abraham, “Lord” is stated repeatedly, except for those few places where there is good reason.
Firstly, in the vision beginning, “And when Abram was ninety years old and nine, the Lord appeared unto Abram and said unto him: I am God Almighty …”<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">4. Gen. 17:1.</i> Having begun with “Lord” in accordance with the principle I mentioned—that Abraham possessed this important knowledge—the writer of the Torah continues the rest of the vision without mentioning “Lord” again, for the vision refers to material things appealing to the imagination, such as Abraham’s becoming the patriarch for many peoples through Isaac and Ishmael; and also the command to make the sign in his flesh, and that of his descendants. For this reason “God” is used, for being equivocal, it refers to the Heavenly Spheres as well as the imaginative faculty predominating in every prophet, except Moses.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">5. <i>Guide</i> II:36.</i> This is certainly the case when those prophets perceive that which is of a material nature and imagined, as has been explained. The Torah is likewise precise in its use of <i>Shaddai</i> (“Almighty”), which, being an adjective similar to <i>davvai</i>, refers to someone victorious (alone) whenever | large numbers of people would (ordinarily) be necessary, as has been explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">6. See below chap. X, n. 3, where this name is discussed.</i> It is the same in the Lord’s words to Jacob, and in those of Isaac to Jacob (when he said), “and God Almighty bless thee and make thee fruitful and multiply thee.”<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">7. Gen. 28:3.</i> It is likewise in Jacob’s words to Joseph, (when saying) “God Almighty appeared unto me at Luz in the land of Canaan and said unto me …”<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">8. Gen. 48:3.</i>
In this verse “walk before me …” in the context of Abraham, the use of the <i>hitpa‘el</i> stem teaches us that the intended meaning of this commandment is to things of a corporeal nature, belonging to the imaginative faculty. Similarly, the occurrence twice of “God” in the story of the smiting of Sodom,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">9. Gen. 19:29.</i> where there was an overflow of fire and brimstone that was similar to (Noah’s) flood with its overflow of water, teaches us that (the Heavenly Spheres) are the proximate and essential cause of everything. Now “Lord” is used most of the time to teach us that the Lord in His glory is the ultimate cause of all the actions and powers of the Heavenly Spheres. In the story of Abraham with Abimelech, when it is related that God spoke to Abimelech, the name “God” (<i>’Elokim</i>) is used,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">10. Gen. 20:6.</i> for he only believed in the Heavenly Spheres, and his (prophetic) faculty was exclusively an expression of the imaginative faculty. It appears that the latter consideration is mentioned even regarding Abraham himself.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">11. Abraham likewise relied on his imaginative faculty for his prophetic visions, for only Moses’ prophecy excluded the use of that faculty.</i> However, in the recovery of Abimelech it says, “for the Lord had fast closed up all the wombs”<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">12. Gen. 20:18.</i> in order to prevent our erring and to ensure our belief that the Lord is the primary cause of all. Similarly, later regarding Abraham there is the precise beginning of “And the Lord remembered Sarah …”<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">13. Gen. 21:1.</i> Thus, because of the circumcision (that was to be commanded), it states, “as God had commanded him,”<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">14. Gen. 21:4.</i> in a usage of the name that is similar to that mentioned in the earlier vision. We can certainly note the precision of “God (<i>’Elokim</i>) hath made laughter for me,”<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">15. Gen. 21:6.</i> which does not use “the Lord.” (This usage is correct) for it is not correct to attribute laughter or a similar attribute (to the Lord, but rather)<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">16. This insertion seems necessary to make sense of the present passage. It is only a tentative suggestion.</i> to the world of Separate Intelligences. There is a similar precision in “And God said unto Abraham, ‘let it not be grievous in thy sight because of the lad and because of thy bondwoman,’ ”<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">17. Gen. 21:12.</i> for such matters of the imagination, including offspring, can only be properly associated with the imaginative faculty,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">18. In commenting on Prov. 17:6, Kaspi says that while there is a command to be fruitful and multiply, it is not one of the more important ones.</i> which is an equivocal term for the name “God.” This is true of material and imagined matters in general, for the imagination is corporeal or material.
The statement regarding Hagar “And God opened her eyes”<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">19. Gen. 21:19.</i> —is surely similar to that which was said of Eve.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">20. Gen. 3:7 records that God opened the eyes of Adam and Eve to see beyond corporeal matters, as explained in the <i>Guide</i> I:2.</i> Regarding what is said of Rebecca—“And the Lord said unto her”<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">21. Gen. 25:23. According to the preceding point, the tetragrammaton would be misplaced here, for the context is of childbirth and other material matters.</i> —there is no problem, for | it is not always and forever necessary to be precise. Sometimes the Torah is exact, and at other times it is inexact, as Maimonides wrote regarding the fifth cause of contradiction as found in the Torah and Prophets.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">22. Here Kaspi alludes to the <i>Guide</i> : Introduction to the first part, which states that on occasion the Torah does not use the exact terminology, instead leaving such precision to another context in the Torah. This principle of Kaspi’s exegesis is discussed in Chapter 2 above. In MK II 67, however, Kaspi prefers to explain that the tetragrammaton here alludes to the Lord as the most distant mover of all speech.</i> Accordingly, occasionally the Torah will be careful to use “God” on account of His being the immediate cause, while at other times “Lord” appears on account of His being the distant mover of all. Reliable evidence of this usage is “And the Lord said to the children of Israel,”<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">23. Judg. 10:11.</i> (which conveys the truth that) in any case He is the mediate speaker.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">24. This means that the Lord spoke to them through some intermediate speaker, for it is not conceivable that there was another revelation to the entire nation as there had been at Sinai.</i>
At the binding of Isaac the use of “God” at the outset, in the command to sacrifice his son, was most appropriate, for (that command) was not apposite of the Lord. For this reason, when preventing the act, the Torah states, “the angel of the Lord”<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">25. Gen. 22:11. Kaspi discusses this event and the names used, in greater detail below chap. XIV, pp. 230–231.</i> and not “the angel of God.” This is because such prevention becomes obvious when one perceives the existence of the world of Separate Intelligences, and clings to that world.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">26. Kaspi means that one who has knowledge of the world of Separate Intelligences knows that such a sacrifice is an abomination.</i> When the Torah refers to Abraham’s act, it is careful to say, “thou art a God-fearing man, seeing thou hast not withheld thy son, thy only son …,”<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">27. Gen. 22:12.</i> for the sacrifice of his son was because he feared God, i.e., the Heavenly Spheres.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">28. Kaspi means that had Abraham sacrificed Isaac, it would have been consistent with the prevalent mentality, which believed exclusively in <i>’Elokim</i>.</i> Such an act was also the result of the imagination, for such was and still is, the custom of the nations in making offerings and sacrifices.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">29. Kaspi’s critical approach to sacrifice is more clearly spelled out in the context of the binding of Isaac below in chap. XIV.</i> Furthermore when interrupted (by the angel, Abraham) said, “ <i>’Adonai yir’eh</i>,”<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">30. Gen. 22:14, meaning “the Lord seeth,” using the tetragrammaton.</i> as opposed to the previous “God will provide Himself …” spoken during the preparation.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">31. Gen. 22:8.</i>
When Laban and Bethuel answered Eliezer, saying, “the thing proceedeth from the Lord,”<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">32. Gen. 24:50.</i> even though they knew only of “God,” (they mentioned “Lord”) because Eliezer the servant of Abraham had attributed everything to the Lord, irr saying, “O Lord, the God of my master Abraham,”<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">33. Gen. 24:42.</i> as with all his statements. Similarly Abimelech said to Isaac, “We saw plainly that the Lord was with thee,”<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">34. Gen. 26:28.</i> i.e., “the Lord who is your God in some way gives you strength and success.”
Regarding Isaac, first it is written, “God blessed Isaac his son,”<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">35. Gen. 25:11.</i> for (at that young age) he had not yet experienced divine (revelation). Yet from that point on, only “Lord” is mentioned in his context. It is the same regarding Rebecca, his wife.
In the stories of Jacob, however, the Torah always uses “Lord,” for he was more complete than was Isaac. Thus, unlike Isaac, who had not been as | complete as Abraham,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">36. Kaspi says that in the following verses, which refer to Isaac, the memory and merits of Abraham are invoked in order to increase the well-being of Isaac. This indicates that Abraham was the greater of the two.</i> it is not said of Jacob, “because that Abraham hearkened to my voice,”<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">37. Gen. 26:5.</i> or “for my servant Abraham’s sake.”<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">38. Gen. 26:24.</i> The exception (where “God” appears in the stories of Jacob) is “the house of God,”<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">39. Gen. 28:17.</i> and that is on account of its being followed by “and this is the gate of heaven.” Jacob said that because he recognized that the place was conducive to prophecy and perception because of the celestial cause that predominated there, as Ibn Ezra noted.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">40. Ibn Ezra says on this verse: “This is the house of God—in which a person can pray in time of need, for his prayer will be heard on account of its being a special place.”</i> He continued to say, “If God will be with me …”<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">41. Gen. 28:20.</i> (using <i>’Elokim</i>) in accordance with the opinion of our Torah that the world of Separate Intelligences is the first cause.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">42. As explained above, <i>’Elokim</i> refers to the upper two worlds, which are here referred to by Kaspi as <i>ha-sekhel ha-nifrad</i> (“the separate intellect”), which as an entity influences the events of the sublunar world below them.</i> The proof of this is provided by the opening verse of the Torah, “In the beginning God created …” as I have explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">43. See above chap. II, n. 1.</i> When Jacob’s wives speak, and when Jacob speaks to them, they mention “God,”<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">44. Gen. 31:5, 9, 11, 16.</i> for that is appropriate for them, as we have already mentioned. This is especially the case as they are the daughters of (the pagan) Laban, as is proved by Rachel when she coveted the teraphim.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">45. Gen. 31:19.</i> When on occasion they do mention “Lord,”<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">46. Gen. 29:32–33, 30:24.</i> it is only because they learnt this from Jacob. Notice the precision regarding Laban when the Torah says, “and God came to Laban,”<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">47. Gen. 31:24.</i> similar to what was written of Abimelech,<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">48. Gen. 20:3.</i> in accordance with his confused belief in the power of the Heavenly Sphere and that of the imagination. Laban said to Jacob, “the God of your father spoke unto me yesternight saying …,”<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">49. Gen. 31:29.</i> for how could the god of Laban assist Jacob when (Jacob) did not believe in him. Similarly he said, “the Lord watch between me and thee,”<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">50. Gen. 31:49.</i> in accordance with Jacob’s belief. Furthermore Laban made Jacob swear by the Lord, for Jacob feared Him, and not God. In summarizing in conclusion, Laban said, “The God of Abraham and the God of Nahor … judge betwixt us …”<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">51. Gen. 31:53.</i> whereas “Jacob swore by the Fear of his father Isaac.”<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">52. Ibid.</i> Jacob uses “God” when saying “and the angels of God met him,”<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">53. Gen. 32:2.</i> and “this is God’s camp,”<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">54. Ibid.</i> as well as “I have seen God face to face”<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">55. Gen. 32:28.</i> and “for thou hast striven with God,”<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">56. Gen. 32:20.</i> for all of them are in a prophetic use of the imagination.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">57. He means that they take place in a prophetic vision, which with the exception of Moses, necessarily involves the imaginative faculty.</i> It is similar with “And God said unto Jacob,”<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">58. Gen. 35:1.</i> | and “there God was revealed unto him,”<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">59. Gen. 35:7.</i> as well as “and God appeared unto Jacob again”<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">60. Gen. 35:9.</i> and “where God spoke with him.”<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">61. Gen. 35:15.</i>
Certainly (this usage of “God”) is true of Joseph,<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">62. Gen. 39:9, 40:8, 41:16.</i> for his prophetic faculty utilized dreams, especially when the composite image concerned corporeal and imaginative matters. This completes the Book of Genesis.
It is well known that the primary concern of Exodus is with Moses, starting, “And Moses was keeping the flock …”<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">63. Exod. 3:1.</i> Notice the care in ordering that episode, for initially it states “the mountain of God,”<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">64. Ibid.</i> for that mountain was where He always appeared. Later, at the onset of Moses’ vision he perceived an angel.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">65. Exod. 3:2.</i> Later yet, having gone higher, it is written, “When the Lord saw.”<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">66. Exod. 3:4.</i> Nonetheless when saying “Draw not nigh hither,”<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">67. Ibid.</i> the words are attributed not to the Lord but to God, for it is only later, after “put off thy shoes,” that we find “and the Lord said …”<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">68. Exod. 3:7.</i> Again later, having already perceived (the Lord), Moses’ perception was weakened at the hiding (of the Lord), and he shrank back at the prospect of accepting the Lord’s charge. Consequently it says, “And Moses said unto God, ‘who am I that I should go unto Pharaoh.’ ”<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">69. Exod. 3:11.</i> This resulted from the weakness of the imaginative faculty, which Moses at that moment perceived as not having been completely purified.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">70. Kaspi explains elsewhere that Moses went through a developmental period, wherein he invoked the wrath of God. The present episode serves to illustrate the earlier stage. See AM 46 ff.</i> Similarly, when it is later mentioned that His name was hidden from Moses, it states, “And Moses said unto God.”<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">71. Exod. 3:13.</i> Furthermore, Moses did not know that “God” is equivocal, proof being that when the Lord answered him, He did not inform Moses that His name was “I am that I am” as well as “the Lord.”<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">72. In these verses it was necessary for Him to omit the tetragrammaton, for at that point Moses was not aware of that name. Consequently only <i>’Elokim</i> is used.</i> For this reason (of Moses’ ignorance) it was correct to write, “And God said … unto Moses.”
From that point and on, however, (only) “Lord” is used. It is only as explained regarding “And God said unto Moses” that we can understand “I am the Lord”<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">73. Exod. 6:3; i.e., it was necessary for Him to affirm the tetragrammaton because there might still have been lingering doubts in Moses’ mind.</i> as making the expression more acceptable. Subsequently, the Torah consistently uses “Lord” up to “Now Jethro heard.”<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">74. Exod. 18:1.</i> | In the context of Jethro, “God” is used, for he was a gentile.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">75. Ibid., but in vv. 9–10 the tetragrammaton is used.</i> (“God”) is likewise mentioned by Moses in reference to the masses and their statutes, in saying, “I make them know the statutes of God and His laws,”<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">76. Exod. 18:6.</i> and not “the statutes of the Lord.” At the gathering (of the people at Mount Sinai) Moses was careful (to write “God”), for he did not want to associate his ascent with “the Lord,” only with “God.”<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">77. Exod. 19:3.</i> Similarly it says, “And Moses brought forth the people out of the camp to meet God,”<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">78. Exod. 19:17.</i> when they approached the Heavenly Sphere together, in a physical sense,—something which cannot be true of the Lord, who has no place. Yet it does say there, “And the Lord came down upon Mount Sinai,”<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">79. Exod. 19:20.</i> (and not “God”) for it was there that the influence of the world of Separate Intelligences was in effect over Moses.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">80. The tetragrammaton here is equivocal, referring to the world of Separate Intelligences as a whole.</i> Regarding the Ten Commandments the Torah is even more precise when it says, “And God spoke all these words saying.”<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">81. Exod. 20:1, at the outset of the Ten Commandments.</i> The reason is that while we find “And the Lord spoke” in several places in reference to Moses, in this case it is known that God spoke with all Israel. Therefore “And the Lord said”<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">82. Exod. 20:19.</i> is an accurate allusion to the world of Separate Intelligences. Nonetheless the speaker was God (the Lord<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">83. It would appear that this is an error in the MS, which should read as referring to the tetragrammaton.</i>), as is evident from the portion <i>Va’etḥanan</i> in the verse, “The Lord spoke with you face to face … I stood between the Lord and you …”<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">84. Deut. 5:4–5.</i> Notice how careful these words are in informing us of the true identity of that speaker and in attributing those words to the Lord in His glory, for it was He who was after all the distant speaker. In addition Moses says of himself “but Moses drew near unto the thick darkness where God (not “the Lord”) was,”<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">85. Exod. 20:18.</i> for such is the style in Hebrew.
From that point and on, throughout the Book of Exodus, as well as the Book of Leviticus, only “the Lord” is written.
In the Book of Numbers, however, it is written, “and the people spoke against their God against Moses,”<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">86. Num. 21:5.</i> it being impossible to relate their action to a Lord that they did not know, or know of. Evidence of this is provided by the fact that even at Mount Sinai, where Moses wanted foremost to have them believe in the Lord, they said at the end, “but let not God speak with us”<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">87. Exod. 20:16.</i> instead of “the Lord.”
In the story of Balaam it is written initially, “And God came unto Balaam and said,”<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">88. Num. 22:9.</i> for that is what he believed.<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">89. He means that Balaam did not believe in the world of Separate Intelligences.</i> In addition his prophetic faculty was by virtue of his imagination, even though Balaam and Balak mentioned the name “Lord” several times<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">90. Num. 23:3, 8, 27; 24:11.</i> — | that was only because He is the God of Israel. Every nation, besides knowing the names of its gods, knows also the names of the gods of other nations, such as “Nergal,”<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">91. II Kings 17:30, “And the men of Babylon made Succoth-benoth, and the men of Cuth made Nergal, and the men of Hamath made Ashima …” referring to the gods made by the nations.</i> “Ashima,”<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">92. Ibid.</i> and the others. This is like our comments regarding the words of Abimelech and Laban in speaking with the patriarchs.
In the Book of Deuteronomy only “Lord” is used. Most precise of all is the statement made of the Lord, “And He made them move to and fro in the desert forty years.”<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">93. Num. 32:13, lit. “he caused them to move.”</i> After all, it is well known that Moses our teacher was a more immediate cause of their movement than any external factor, while the Lord was the distant cause. One can conclude that throughout the Torah and Scripture there are several intermediary causes, whether the most distant or more immediate,<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">94. In <i>Menorat Kesef</i> (AS II 86), Kaspi quotes other examples of such terminology, such as “So Solomon built the house” (I Kings 6:14); “And the Lord said … I will write upon the tablets …” (Exod. 34:1).</i> while there is nothing at all equivocal in the name “Lord.”<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">95. This means that there is no implication that the Lord Himself carried out His will—He only initiated it.</i> The name (“Lord”) retains its original meaning always, even though all things are attributed to it, for it is the cause and (first) mover of everything, from a distance. Maimonides explained this in the final chapter of Part II (of the <i>Guide</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">96. <i>Guide</i> II:48.</i>

Chapter 4

In my opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">1. The subject of the names of God is analyzed above in Chapter 3. In AK I 124 Kaspi refers the reader to the <i>Gevia‘ Kesef</i> in order to understand the subject of the names of God, saying that “whoever possesses the <i>Gevia‘</i> will understand perhaps one-tenth [of that subject].”</i> there are three names exclusively applied to the Lord. This means that they are primary, and not derivative, appellations. Even though some of them are on the pattern used for derivative names, they (themselves) are not derivative. These are the tetragrammaton, i.e., <i>yod he’ vav he’</i> ; the name <i>yod he’</i>, which is one-half of the former; as well as <i>’Ehyeh</i>. This is in accordance with what I have written of this in the <i>Menorat Kesef</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">2. In the <i>Menorat Kesef</i> (AS II 94–95) Kaspi says that God has three names distinguishing Him from every other existent. These three, as mentioned here as well, are mentioned in <i>Guide</i> 1:61–63. What follows in the <i>Gevia‘ Kesef</i> expands considerably upon the discussion in the <i>Menorat Kesef</i>.</i> In brief I will say here that each of these three names is the special name of the Prime Cause, and that not one of these (names) can be associated with the world of Separate Intelligences, and certainly not with what is below them. Even though they are all primary names, what is unique and special about each of them is the following.
The tetragrammaton is unique and special in that, even though it has some meaning and interpretation according to its letters,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">3. The letters of the tetragrammaton are those used to indicate existence.</i> if (it) is like <i>yigleh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">4. Lit. “uncover.”</i> and <i>yirbeh</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">5. Lit. “multiply”; i.e., the tetragrammaton is, like these, a final weak verb in the imperfect.</i> it is different and distinct from every name because of the prohibition of reading it (according to its letters). Thus, because it has an interpretation (<i>perush</i>) as well as a distinctness (<i>hefresh</i>), it is known for both reasons as the <i>shem ha-meforash</i>, for the root <i>parash</i> has both these meanings, which are really one.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">6. Maimonides (<i>Guide</i> I:61) explains the name as follows: “This name gives a clear, unequivocal indication of His essence,” unlike all the other names of God, which are equivocal and signify actions, the like of which exist as our own actions. This is reflected in Kaspi’s first explanation that <i>perush</i> means “explanation.”</i> This is the precise and special nature of this name—that with respect to its letters it has a meaning, but that with respect to its reading it has no meaning, except in the Temple.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">7. The priestly blessing invoked this name, cf. <i>Num. Rabbah</i> 11:21. It was also invoked on the Day of Atonement ten times by the high priest. See BT <i>Yoma</i> 39b.</i> And in the Temple, when it is pronounced, it has no independent meaning, | but only (a meaning) by way of allusion that cannot be measured (with precision). The name can be read in one of two ways, or in both of them together, one after the other, according to how the Men of the Great Assembly pointed it in the Bible. One was with a <i>shva’</i> under the <i>yod</i> and a <i>ḳameṣ</i> under the <i>vav</i>, with a <i>naḥ</i> under the two <i>he’</i> letters.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">8. This pointing is found in the vast majority of cases, occurring wherever the tetragrammaton appears alone, or when followed by <i>’Elokim</i>.</i> The other was with a <i>shva’</i> under the <i>yod</i>, a <i>ḥolem</i> under the first <i>he’</i>, and a <i>ḥireḳ</i> under the <i>vav</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">9. For this usage see Gen. 15:2, 8 and Deut. 3:24, 9:26. It is also found frequently in Amos and Ezekiel.</i> There is no name in Hebrew with a construction similar to this. The latter formulation was only made by the Men of the Great Assembly when it was preceded<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">10. There are five instances of this: Hab. 3:19; Ps. 109:21, 140:8, 141:8, 68:21.</i> by <i>’Adonai</i>, spelled with an <i>’alef-dalet</i>, as occurred at the vision of the covenant regarding Abraham,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">11. Gen. 15:2, 8.</i> and in <i>Va’etḥanan</i> with Moses.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">12. Deut. 3:24.</i> This strange pointing of the ineffable name was necessary, for (in its usual pointing) it is read by people as the spelling <i>’alef-dalet</i>, and how could they read <i>’Adonai ’Adonai</i> (as if the same name were written twice). Therefore it is read as if it were written <i>’Adonai ’Elokim</i>. The punctuation of the ineffable name has no meaning, and neither does its pronunciation, yet it does possess meaning according to its letters, for if we were (permitted) to read it at will, its meaning would be “exists” and “causes to exist,” which would (not) be exclusive to Him.
They not only made the ineffable name exclusive to Him, they also made His attributes exclusive, through the use and pronunciation of <i>’Adonai</i>. For it at no time appears with a <i>ḥireḳ</i> under the <i>nun</i> (in which case it would mean “my master”), but only appears with a <i>ḳameṣ</i> so as to indicate an absolute master, as in <i>davvai</i> or <i>Shaddai</i>, (the latter) being derived from <i>shoded</i> (“destruction”).<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">13. Here Kaspi follows Ibn Ezra rather than Maimonides, who had explained <i>Shaddai</i> as coming from <i>she-dai</i> in <i>Guide</i> I:63. In the present context Kaspi addúces the form <i>Shaddai</i> and emphasizes that it comes from the <i>shoded</i> in order to demonstrate the existence of the nonpronominal suffix <i>ai</i>. This claim, however, is offset by the second parallel mentioned here— <i>davvai</i> —which implies that the root is indeed <i>shaddai</i>. The subject is discussed below in chap. X, n. 3, and in Chapter 3 above.</i> Proof (for the latter) is the verse “As destruction (<i>kāshud</i>) from the Almighty (<i>mi-Shaddai</i>) shall it come.”<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">14. Isa. 13:6; Joel 1:5.</i> Even though this form can be found in the Hebrew usage in that <i>’Adonai</i> could mean “my lords,”<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">15. Kimḥi, <i>Mikhlol</i>, ed. I. Rittenberg (Jerusalem, 1966), p. 12a, suggests that <i>’adonai</i> (with <i>pataḥ</i>) can mean “my lords,” by way of an honorific form of address.</i> as has been claimed in this matter,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">16. In the <i>Guide</i> I:61 there is the following passage: “all other names give their names in an equivocal way … even [<i>’Adonai</i>] is derived from a term signifying lordship … for these words are emphatic and of a general character applicable also to other beings.”</i> the intent of the Men of the Great Assembly is that it be understood without the adjective “my,” but alone, as in <i>’adon</i> (“master”) or <i>Shaddai</i> (“Almighty”), That is why Maimonides was careful to compare (<i>’Adonai</i>) to “Sarai,” the wife of Abram, saying in the <i>Guide</i> I:61 that such endings “are emphatic and of a general character,” for every specific reference to “my” or “ours” gives a particular (connotation excluding other referents). (Nowhere in relation to God) does the Bible state <i>’adoni</i> with a <i>ḥireḳ</i> under the <i>nun</i>, even when David and the prophets exclaimed, “the Lord (<i>’Adonai</i>), the Lord (tetragrammaton) of hosts.”<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">17. Isa. 3:15.</i> Notice how the writer of the Torah was careful to convey the equivocal meaning of <i>’Adonai</i> when it has the <i>ḳameṣ</i> under the <i>nun</i>, for it can be understood either as “Lord” (<i>’adon</i>) or as “my lord” (<i>’adoni)</i>. Thus when He wanted to mention the wife of Abraham, and to allude to his lordship in general, it says| “thou shalt not call her name Sarai, but Sarah shall her name be,”<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">18. Gen. 17:15.</i> i.e., one could err regarding “Sarai” by explaining it as containing “my,” whereas with “Sarah” one cannot err. This is similar to the prophets’ writing <i>ha-’Adon</i> (“the Lord”),<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">19. Here “the Lord” is written as <i>ha-’Adon</i> without the polysemous suffix <i>-ai</i>.</i> as I have explained.
What emerges from all that they wrote is that the tetragrammaton refers exclusively to the Lord, because of its unique character, as I have described it.
It is also evident that <i>yod he’</i> refers exclusively to Him, for being one-half of the ineffable name, it has no meaning at all. Certainly its meaning is not “eternal,” as Maimonides thinks it is,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">20. In the <i>Guide</i> I:61 the tetragrammaton is said to indicate a necessary existence. In <i>Guide</i> I:63, in explaining “I am that I am,” this is repeated, as well as in the case of the bigrammaton, which “refers to the notion of the eternity of existence.”</i> without diminishing from his glory. (After all) nothing like it is to be found anywhere in the Hebrew language. This name is special in that everyone pronounces it, but no one understands it.
<i>’Ehyeh ’asher ’Ehyeh</i> (“I am that I am”) refers exclusively to the Lord in a different way. It has meaning as written and as pointed. Like the (tetragrammaton) it refers exclusively to Him, and is a hylic name.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">21. In Heb. <i>shem hiyyulani</i>, meaning “underived.”</i> This is because the qualifying clause (“that I am”) is identical with the subject (“I am”), giving the appearance of a mockery.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">22. He means that the statement would appear to be a tautology.</i> Yet it is not a mockery, for the repetition alludes to the fact that we cannot perceive Him in any specific manner,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">23. For further discussion of this see above Chapter 3.</i> which is the meaning of “for man shall not see me and live”<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">24. Exod. 33:20.</i> and “my face shall not be seen.”<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">25. Exod. 33:23.</i>
This being the case, what these three names have in common is that each is necessarily a primary name, for every derivative name must be composed of two elements, a predicate relating to a subject, even though it may not say so specifically. Because only the Lord is independent of such duality, whether from above or below, He is unique in having a special primary name, which is not derived. These three names share this (characteristic), each of them with its own special properties, as we have explained.

Chapter 5

Notice how the truth is its own witness and consistent in every respect.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">1. In <i>Ṭirat Kesef</i> (MK I 83) Kaspi explains: “the meaning of this is that one side bears witness to the other side. But why should I quote other philosophers, while omitting the words of the perfect philosophers of our people, who said, ‘Words of truth are self-evident’ [<i>nikkarin divrei ’emet</i>]” (BT Soṭah 9a). Kaspi’s reference to other philosophers is to the long tradition in Islamic philosophy, starting with Al-Kindi and culminating in Averroës. The latter accepted the parity of philosophy and Scripture as two primary and infallible sources of truth. It is his formulation which is quoted in the present passage, as quoted in G. F. Hourani, <i>On the Harmony of Religion and Philosophy (Kitab Fasl Al-Maqāl</i>) (London: Luzac, 1961), p. 51: “… demonstrative study does not lead to conclusions conflicting with what Scripture has given us; for truth does not oppose truth, but accords with it and bears witness to it.” This passage is also quoted by Falaquera, <i>Sefer Ha-Ma‘alot</i> (Berlin, 1894), p. 12. For a Hebrew translation, see <i>PAAJR XXV</i> (1956), p. 107.</i> The number three is well established among all nations,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">2. He means that the number figures prominently in the theology of many religions.</i> one of the reasons being that it is a complete numeral, possessing a beginning and a middle, as stated by Aristotle in <i>De Caelo</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">3. <i>De Caelo</i> I, 1, 168a: “For as the Pythagoreans say, the world and all that is in it is determined by the number three, since beginning and middle and end give the number of an ‘all,’ and the number they give is the triad.”</i> Consequently our prayers are characterized by the number three.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">4. This is probably in reference to the comments regarding the <i>ḳedushah</i> prayer in chap. I, above pp. 137–138.</i> Therefore the giver of the Torah, in agreeing with this (universality of the number three), enunciated three (divine) names at the beginning of the Torah, | as above.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">5. Cf. above chap. II, p. 138.</i> These (names) are “God,” “the Lord God,” and “the Lord.” Subsequently, at the auspicious encounter of Moses with the Lord during the second forty-day period (at Mount Sinai), it is written “the Lord the Lord God” (Tetragrammaton tetragrammaton <i>’El</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">6. Exod. 34:6.</i> In the priestly blessing “Lord” appears three times.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">7. Num. 6:24–26.</i> Similarly, when the names of God were given, they were three in number: the tetragrammaton,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">8. As Kaspi explained earlier, the tetragrammaton really includes two names, i.e., the full four letters, and the first two alone. This makes up the number three in the present context.</i> “’Ehyeh,” (and the bigrammaton). This also accords with our perception of the world of Intelligences in every respect.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">9. These were explained above in chap. I, pp. 136–137.</i> It is also in accordance with our conception of the first Intelligence, as explained above.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">10. In the <i>Menorat Kesef</i> (AS II 82–83) he explains the details of this doctrine, where the first intelligence (the <i>mishneh ha-ḳarov</i>) necessarily has two perceptions: of itself and of the Lord. All lower intelligences have three perceptions: of themselves, of the immediately preceding intelligence, and of the Lord.</i> This is not in the sense that the world (of Separate Intelligences) can be divided conceptually into three parts, the Primary One, the Secondary Ones (the Intelligences), and the Active Intellect—but that rather each of the Intelligences, with the exception of the first, has at least three components, in that it consists of its perceptions of the Primary One, as well as of the immediately preceding Intelligence, as well as (its perception) of itself.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">11. This is explained in the <i>Guide</i> I:69.</i> Even the Primary One Himself has a threefold aspect in that He is cause of the assorted Intelligences in three ways: as efficient cause, formal cause, and final cause.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">12. Cf. above chap. I, p. 137, n. 14.</i> In further explanation of all this, Isaiah wrote, “And one called to another and said, ‘Holy, holy, holy, is the Lord of hosts …’ ”<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">13. Isa. 6:3.</i> The exact meaning of “hosts” in this context is the Separate Intelligences, while elsewhere “hosts” refers to the Heavenly Spheres, and all Israel. They are all true.
(The Men of the Great Assembly) arranged certain of our prayers to clarify that the threefold repetition (of “holy”) in Isaiah refers to the world of Separate Intelligences, whether to that entire world or only the highest part.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">14. The term “Separate Intelligences” could accordingly refer to that world as a whole or to its highest component, the Lord Himself. Cf. chap. III, n. 42.</i> Thus, when addressing the Lord, the (prayer) says, “they attest three times to your holiness,” and “those who treble their sanctification of you.”<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">15. This is according to the Sephardic version of the <i>ḳedushah</i> prayer.</i> I have also spoken at length on this in the <i>Menorat Kesef</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">16. The subject is discussed in general in the <i>Menorat Kesef</i> (AS II 80–95) without any reference to these verses or prayers.</i> From my words at the beginning of the work, one can understand the verbal similarity between us and the Christians, in that with regard to the trinity they speak of “Father,” “Son,” and “Spirit,” asserting that they are both three and one, which is similar (to what we say). This is all written in the Torah, (such as) when the Lord is described as a father, most remarkably when it says “(thou didst forget God) that bore thee,”<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">17. Deut. 32:18.</i> for this of necessity means that He has a child. This is especially so in view of the verse “Ye are the children of the Lord your God.”<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">18. Deut. 14:1.</i> Furthermore it is written with regard to the Active Intellect: “the spirit of God,”<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">19. Gen. 1:2.</i> corresponding to “the spirit.” Yet | we are really far-removed from the Christians in opinions despite an external similarity in phrases and in speech, even though some individuals among them are similar to us (in opinions).<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">20. This is but one of several statements that Kaspi makes indicating his concerns regarding Christological claims and Biblical exegesis. Many of the verses mentioned in the present chapter are included among the stock Christian arguments on these issues related to the trinity, and it is likely that their mention here is connected to such Christian claims. In this regard, see above Chapter 2, pp. 69 ff. for its impact on Kaspi’s exegesis, as well as Chapter 3, pp. 95–96 as to the latter years of Kaspi’s literary activity. It should be noted in the present context that while Kaspi appears here to countenance the idea of common approaches to exegesis, as a rule he is scathingly critical of Christological exegesis and polemics. For a list of references to Christianity in Kaspi’s writings, see W. Bacher, “Aus der Bibelexegese Joseph Ibn Kaspis,” <i>MGWJ</i> LVI (1912), pp. 209–217.</i>

Chapter 6

What is written in this work up to this point, as well as what is written in <i>Mizraḳ Kesef</i> and <i>Menorat Kesef</i>, has resolved the first sixteen questions that we enumerated in <i>Kesef Sigim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">1. On the interrelationship of these works and of the <i>Gevia‘ Kesef</i>, see above pp. 125–132.</i> Therefore I will now proceed to resolve the others one at a time, by writing my opinion regarding the seventeenth question, which embodies two doubts. Regarding the first,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">2. The question, it appears, was as follows: Noah was commanded to take seven pairs of every species of pure beast, two pairs of each species of impure beast, and seven pairs of each species of fowl of the air—would not a uniform number of one or two pairs have sufficed to preserve each species, as in the case of the impure beasts? If the purpose was for sacrifice when disembarking (Gen. 8:20), why require seven pairs if only one of each kind was sacrificed?</i> it is clear that even though Noah attempted to bring beasts and fowl into the ark to save some seed from each (species) through growth, before the floodwaters came, nonetheless when the rain began and continued for several days, several of the beasts and fowl came in the door on their own<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">3. Gen. 7:8, “Of clean beasts and beasts that are not clean, and of fowls and everything that creepeth upon the ground, there <i>went</i> in two and two unto Noah into the ark …”</i> for shelter and protection from the pouring rain, for the ark door, which was on the side, remained open for four, five, or ten days, (possibly) more. Certainly after the waters had lifted the ark and raised it above ground level, Noah must have forcibly pushed off certain beasts and fowl, for he needed no more than a (certain) number to preserve the species, while he did not have a lot of food in the ark to support and manage them (all). It is not necessary to take the numbers “seven” or “two” literally, for “two” refers to the minimum necessary to maintain the species, i.e., male and female. The number “seven” connotes “many,” (as many) as Noah saw fit (to take), for the Hebrew language does often refer to a multitude with the number “seven.”<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">4. For the significance and widespread use of the number seven in antiquity, see J. Lewy and H. Lewy, “The Origin of the Week and the Oldest West-Asiatic Calendar,” <i>HUCA</i> XVII (1942), p. 22. Maimonides (<i>Guide</i> II:29) states: “for the Hebrews denote multiplicity by the number seven.” See below chap. XXI, n. 7.</i> The reason that the Lord commanded Noah to take many pure beasts was because man has great need for nourishment, so Noah and his children could eat them in the ark. Furthermore, because (the pure beasts) would be continuously fit for consumption later on, it was necessary that many of them emerge from the ark. In addition, when Noah emerged it says “he took of them for an offering,” but not from the impure beasts.
Regarding the fowl, many were necessary, both pure and impure. As for the pure, (many were necessary) for the same reason as the many pure beasts,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">5. Many fowl were required for consumption.</i> and as for the impure, many were necessary, for undoubtedly sickness and death prevailed among the fowl in the ark—for being accustomed to fly, they would die when confined in the ark, and only a few survive, as we can see for ourselves | when birds are sent from this country by ship across the sea to the king of Ishmael, as a gift.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">6. Kaspi was familiar with the lore of the sea from his own extensive travels. Another example of this is found below chap. IX, p. 191. On the general use of this principle of exegesis by Kaspi, see above pp. 61–63.</i>
Furthermore, because fowl can fly a long distance to concealed places, many of them flew to and entered the ark, whether with or against Noah’s will. This solves the doubt engendered by the question.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">7. Cf. above n. 2.</i>
Regarding the resolution of the second doubt as mentioned there,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">8. From what follows, it appears that the doubt was caused by the verse “The Lord smelled the sweet savor, and the Lord said in His heart, ‘I will not again curse the ground …’” (Gen. 8:20–22). Are not sacrifices in general to be avoided, as reason would dictate, and why was the Lord so affected by the particular sacrifice offered by Noah after he descended from the ark?</i> and the others in <i>Kesef Sigim</i> included in the seventeenth question, what I wrote in the <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">9. In <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i> (MK II 47) Kaspi advocated the superiority of prayer over sacrifice, and even over the building of sanctuaries. Cf. <i>Guide</i> II:32 for a similar statement.</i> and the <i>Ṭirat Kesef</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">10. <i>Ṭirat Kesef</i> (MK I 160).</i> suffices to answer it. Nonetheless, writing with great brevity, I can say here that the giver of the Torah wrote Genesis in a manner similar to Job; i.e., he wrote narratives describing passing matters,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">11. He means that the truths of theoretical and practical philosphy are not presented in a discursive analytic style, but rather occur in the lives of individuals and nations as described by the use of anecdote.</i> determined to inform us in Genesis of the righteous and their activities in all their details. This is particularly with regard to the patriarchs and their descendants. This (was done) because most people strive to imitate their forefathers. For this reason Moses in the Torah told us to offer sacrifices, even though in truth they are an abomination. This, however, is something that it is not proper for the masses to know, (for sacrifices) are necessary to maintain a community.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">12. On Kaspi’s attitude to the masses and their understanding of Scripture, see above pp. 12 ff, 118, 130 ff.</i> This is especially (necessary) when the opinion of the masses is that sacrifice is most desired by the Lord. Under no circumstances, however, should (sacrifices be offered) to the Heavenly Spheres, but only to the Lord. The same is true of prayer, for even though it is superior to sacrifice, as Maimonides has hinted,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">13. <i>Guide</i> III:32, “Sacrifice pertains to the second intention, whereas invocation, prayer, and modes of worship come closer to the first intention …”</i> nonetheless when we make assembly halls, a Temple, or synagogues, these, like sacrifices, are not necessary in truth. In the final analysis, however, we must admit that our religion does permit sacrifice when to the Lord, and therefore Moses was correct in writing for us, by way of anecdote, of what occurred in earlier times, those things that demonstrate truth to us individuals, and coarse knowledge to the masses, teaching them how to act in relation to the Lord.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">14. Kaspi means that the masses, upon reading these stories, will conclude that sacrifice is desirable, while select individuals will detect the truth that sacrifice is not desired by God. On Kaspi’s method of “detecting the truth” in such matters, see above Chapter 2.</i> Thus, for (the benefit of singular) individuals, when Adam was in the garden of Eden and the Lord told him of | all the animals that he took, Scripture does not mention that He told Adam to sacrifice them.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">15. Gen. 2:19–20.</i> God did not bring them to him for that purpose, but only to see what Adam would name them—i.e., to observe and examine their nature so as to give them a Hebrew name indicating their nature in some way.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">16. Kaspi explains in many places (eg. MK II 296–297; <i>Shulḥan Kesef</i>, fols. 197 ff.) that Hebrew terms reflect the inner nature of the objects they name. For further discussion of this, see above pp. 52 ff.</i> And that is what he did.
In a later passage, in writing of Adam’s three sons, far be it from (the writer of the Torah) to say that the righteous Seth made a sacrifice, as was said of Cain and Abel, the (foolish) enthusiasts.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">17. Gen. 4:3–5. In Hebrew the term is <i>he-ḥasid</i>. Kaspi uses the term pejoratively below on fol. 143a, <i>he-ḥasid shoṭeh</i>.</i> In order to benefit the masses, however, it is written that they (sacrificed) to the Lord, and not to God, as it is written, “He that sacrifices unto God, save unto the Lord only, shall be utterly destroyed.”<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">18. Exod. 22:19.</i> Later, regarding Noah, who was as righteous as Abel, Job and his friends, the Torah was careful not to write that the Lord commanded Noah to sacrifice to Him, and not to write that he should take many animals into the ark for the purpose of making a sacrifice. Instead (the purpose was) to maintain the species, and Noah made the sacrifices of his own accord, as did Abel and Job.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">19. Job 42:1–10.</i> In any case, Noah’s sacrifice is described as made “to the Lord,” and also (resulting in) “the Lord smelled the sweet savor,”<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">20. Gen. 8:20–21.</i> for that is what the masses should believe. Proof of this is provided by the words “for a sweet savor unto the Lord.”<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">21. Lev. 4:31, 17:6; Num. 15:24, 18:17, et al. He means that “a sweet savor unto the Lord” means that those who sacrifice consider it sweet. In addition it is also “unto the Lord.” Here, the Lord smelled what was to Noah a sweet smell.</i> For select individuals, however, the writer of the Torah did not write that the three patriarchs sacrificed to the Lord, but wrote only that they made an altar<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">22. Gen. 13:4, 26:25, 35:7.</i> and a house<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">23. Gen. 33:17.</i> and a pillar.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">24. Gen. 28:18.</i> Generally a structure is erected in commemoration, as is written regarding the Gadites and the Reubenites,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">25. Josh. 22:9–24.</i> and some of the acts of Gideon.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">26. Judg. 6:24.</i> These (structures) remind us that they are to be preferred over making a sacrifice, and the Torah, by omitting to make any mention of sacrifice (by the patriarchs), teaches us that sacrifice is not desirable. Thus neither the patriarchs nor Moses at the altar known as <i>’Adonai-nissi</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">27. Exod. 17:15.</i> offered (sacrifice). When subsequently Moses began to command Israel to make sacrifices, it was commanded to be performed by the young men among them, as it is written, “the young men of the children of Israel.”<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">28. Exod. 24:5, in Heb. <i>na‘arei benei yisra’el</i>.</i> Onkelos translated this as <i>zeṭuṭi benei yisra’el</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">29. This is not found in our editions of Onkelos, where we have instead <i>buḥrei benei yisra’el</i>. The term <i>zeṭuṭi</i> quoted here is found in both Talmuds. In BT <i>Megillah</i> 9a it is related that the seventy-two sages who translated the Bible at the behest of Ptolemy all substituted the word <i>zeṭuṭi</i> for <i>na‘arei</i>. In JT <i>Ta‘anit</i> 4:2 it is related that three Torahs were discovered (II Chron. 34:15), one of which had substituted <i>zeṭuṭi</i> for <i>na‘arei</i>. Kaspi might have had another version of Onkelos or, more likely, confused Onkelos with the translation mentioned in the Talmud. The Targum Yonatan b. Uziel on Exod. 24:11 has <i>zeṭuṭi</i> in translating <i>’aṣilei</i>. Cf. M. Jastrow, <i>Dictionary</i> (New York, 1967), s.v. <i>za‘ṭuṭi</i>.</i> which connotes “youths” in Hebrew. It is clear, for those who can see, that from the way in which sacrifices are described in the Torah, they were (permitted) only by way of necessity, for the reason mentioned by Maimonides.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">30. See <i>Guide</i> III:32, of which this entire section is a reflection.</i> This is certainly true of the description in Jeremiah of child-sacrifice to Baal and Molech,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">31. Jer. 7:31, 19:5.</i> and all the more true of the fact that the writer of the Torah recorded the binding of Isaac, as I wrote in <i>Ṭirat Kesef</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">32. <i>Ṭirat Kesef</i> (MK I 102–103).</i> and <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">33. <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i> (MK II 62).</i> If the Lord grant me length of days, I will gather all (these ideas) in the present work.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">34. See below chaps. XIV and XVIII. The phrase “length of days” implies that the work was written at the end of his life. On this point, see above p. 131.</i>
What I wrote (in <i>Kesef Sigim</i>) regarding Noah and his descendants at the time of the division (of that generation)<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">35. The question implied is: Why was it necessary to record the details of Gen. 10, with its names, families, and places of habitation—of what significance could they be?</i> was occasioned by confusion (in that matter).<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">36. Gen. 10:32–11:9.</i> Just as it is clear what is the benefit gained from the story of the flood in all its details—that, as Maimonides said, by reading the prophetic books we know that God was cognizant of their deeds<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">37. <i>Guide</i> III:50, saying that the flood and Sodom’s fate were recorded to teach us that “there is a God that judges.”</i> (and therefore brought on the flood)—so too, in truth, the entire story (of the divided generation) teaches the same lesson.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">38. We learn that God knows the lives of human beings.</i> How can this be disputed—for we see such things constantly.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">39. He means to say that we see this constantly in Scripture.</i> As for the passing details regarding large numbers and small numbers, and large segments of the earth with small segments—these are but as Maimonides said regarding Job, that whether or not he existed, there are many people like him who do exist.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">40. <i>Guide</i> III:22.</i> This is also true of the story of the uncovering of Noah, and the actions taken by his sons in that regard.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">41. Gen. 9:20–28.</i> (In this regard) I have already written whatever is necessary (to understand this matter) in <i>Ṭirat Kesef</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">42. <i>Ṭirat Kesef</i> (MK I 67): “the choice fruit” of the story of Noah comes at its end, with “cursed be Canaan, a servant of servants,” which establishes that Canaan will be a slave to his brothers and to the nations of the world.</i> and <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">43. <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i> (MK II 37–38).</i> This is likewise true of the narrative of the divided generation.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">44. In <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i> (MK II 39–41) Kaspi explains the importance of Gen. 10–11 as alluding to later events in the history of the Israelites, as recorded in the books of the Prophets and later in <i>Josippon</i>.</i> In <i>Miẓrak Kesef</i> I have already informed you of the meaning of “there is no chronological order in Scripture.” (It means that) it is not always necessary that what precedes in order precede in cause, for sometimes it may not be a cause at all, while at other times it might be a cause but only an accidental one (and not an essential one),<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">45. On the importance which Kaspi attaches to this doctrine in his Scriptural exegesis, see above pp. 46 ff, 114 ff.</i> as is the case when lightning strikes and kills an ox or sheep.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">46. This phrase is found in Averroës’ <i>Epitome of Aristotle’s Logic</i> (Riva di Trento, 1559), p. 52a, “when lightning strikes and an animal dies …”</i> There are many instances of this. It was, however, necessary that the masses understand this story in a manner appropriate to them, i.e., that the world was created in time and <i>ex nihilo</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">47. This alludes to Kaspi’s opinion that Maimonides accepted the Aristotelian doctrine of the Eternity of the universe, so that God’s priority was causal, not temporal. See especially <i>‘Ammudei Kesef</i> (AM 99–101), and Mesch, <i>Studies</i>, pp. 96 ff.</i> Yet (it was not necessary) that they understand it in as crude a manner as they do. Certainly it is not correct for the complete sage to understand (these things) in the same manner as does the absolute fool, and certainly not when it comes to intelligibles, for how can we equate an angel with matter?<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">48. In Heb. <i>ḥomer</i>.
