It is possible that the original reading was <i>ḥamor</i> (i.e., “ass”), with the <i>vav</i> repositioned, providing the smoother “how could an angel be equated with an ass?”</i> Consequently it was necessary to write that the people who left there (in the divided generation) settled (in the land of Shinar), because of the narratives of the Creation, of the flood, and of the ark resting on Mount Ararat,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">49. Gen. 8:4.</i> i.e., in the lands of Babylon and Shinar, as is written regarding Sennacherib.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">50. Isa. 37:38.</i> Even prior to the divided generation, the author of the Torah was careful to write of other lands and languages in the context of the three sons of Noah.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">51. Gen. 10 speaks of various lands and languages even prior to the division in chap. 11.</i> This being the case, the verse “And the whole earth was of one language and of one speech”<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">52. Gen. 11:1.</i> does not refer to “earth” as an all-inclusive term<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">53. The MS is unclear at this point. According to this reading, the meaning is that the area where the following events occurred was a limited one, not covering the entire earth, for there were already many different lands and languages. Such a reading would accord with Kaspi’s explanation (in <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i>, MK II 16–17) of “and there was not a man to till the ground” (Gen. 2:5), where he says that in other places there were men to till the ground. See S. Pines, “Histabberut Ha-Teḳumah Me-Ḥadash shel Medinah Yehudit le-fi Yosef Ibn Kaspi u-le-fi Spinoza,” <i>Iyyun</i> XIV (1963), p. 289, n. 2.</i> … in <i>Kesef Sigim</i>.

Chapter 7

The explanation<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">1. In <i>Ṃasref La-Kesef</i> (MK II 51) Kaspi says that this vision “is explained in the <i>Gevia‘ Kesef</i> …”</i> of Abraham’s first vision, beginning “After these things the word of the Lord came to Abram,”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">2. Gen. 15:1.</i> needs to be written, because this vision is subtle and profound. In the first place, I will say that to understand the vision of the Sundering, (one should know that) the purpose of the Torah in writing all the details of this story was to inform us that this (vision actually) took place involving Abraham our forefather and the Lord; and also to inform us what it was that Abraham said to the Lord and what the Lord said to Abraham.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">3. In the <i>Ṭirat Kesef</i> (MK I 77) he explains that there was no speaking, for it occurred in Abraham’s sleep; their dialogue was comparable to a dream, involving a mental exchange between God and Abraham.</i> | Abraham’s actions occurred literally, in a manner similar to what is recorded in the Book of Job.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">4. He means that both Abraham and Job are described in Scripture as historical personalities.</i> And even though it is possible to say that Job and his friends never existed, the same could not be said of Abraham, Isaac, Jacob, and their descendants.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">5. This was a doctrine espoused by certain Provençal philosophers who allegorized certain passages and personalities in the Bible, most notably those of Abraham and Sarah. For a greater discussion of this, see above pp. 6 ff. and 37.</i> No matter what, we had patriarchs of old, for we did not spring out of the dust.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">6. The question of the historicity of Job occurs in the Talmud (BT <i>Bava‘ Batra‘</i> 15a), where there is an opinion that Job never existed. Maimonides, in <i>Guide</i> III:22, appears to maintain this as an open possibility, and Kaspi likewise leaves the question open. See below chap. XVIII, where this is mentioned again.</i>
Having said this, I will (now) explain this episode accurately. I can affirm that in writing this story, the author of the Torah intended to teach us many important things, (to be learnt from) every statement and every affirmation, as is clear. Thus, in the first place, from the specific statement “in a vision,”<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">7. Here Kaspi notices an apparent redundancy, for this term is not usually found in prophetic visions. On Kaspi’s attitude toward the description of Scriptural details, see below.</i> we learn that wherever it is written that “the word of the Lord came to so-and-so” or “And the word of the Lord came” or “And the Lord said,” and similarly, “And the Lord spoke,” or whenever the Lord’s word is spoken of as coming to a prophet,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">8. He means to include here every prophetic inspiration or appearance of the “holy spirit.”</i> whether it be in a vision or a sight, for they are the same, or whether it be in a dream, it always comes as a force settling on the prophet,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">9. Maimonides (<i>Guide</i> II:36) describes prophecy as “an overflowing from God” that settles on the soul of the prophet.</i> as will be explained in several places.
“I am thy shield, thy reward shall be exceeding great.”<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">10. Gen. 15:1.</i> The intention of these words is to material reward in this world, even though the words “shield” and “reward” could be understood as referring to a spiritual (reward), such as in the world to come, (but such a spiritual sense) is unlikely according to the Hebrew usage.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">11. Among the commentaries, Nachmanides and Gersonides explained the reference as to this world; Kimḥi and Seforno interpret the reward as applied to the next world. In the <i>Ṭirat Kesef</i> (MK I 78) Kaspi explains that promises of reward in the next world are not in need of prophetic confirmation—they can be adduced through intellectual means. Consequently, the present confirmation would refer to the world of the senses.</i> Proof is from the preceding “Fear not, Abram,” for “fear” (<i>yir’ah</i>) in its Hebrew usage can only be used in the context of this-worldly fear, of whatever kind it might be, or else the absence of what is good. Further proof of this is that evidently Abraham took it in the same way, (from his words) “what wilt thou give me since I go hence childless.” God even explained the reward to him: “he that comes forth out of thy own bowels shall be thine heir.”<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">12. V. 4.</i> The complete statement, from “thy reward shall be exceeding great” until “so shall thy seed be,”<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">13. Vv. 1–5.</i> did not add any new information from God, for it is not necessary that the Torah should (always) say something new on account of people’s talk.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">14. On the role of Scriptural redundancies and Kaspi’s doctrine that “the Torah speaks in the language of men,” see above pp. 59 ff.</i>
Now a prophet, or anyone else, should not pray to the Lord to renew His favor, nor should he request reassurance of the Lord’s earlier words. Accordingly, when the Lord had said to Abraham, “thy reward shall be exceeding great,” Abraham requested an explanation of the words | “reward” and “great.” God then explained these terms to him at length, as well as the phrase “I am thy shield,” which was added to them. This is similar to “Hast thou not made a hedge about him, and about his house and about all that he has on every side.?”<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">15. Job 1:10, meaning that God would protect Abraham, as He did for Job.</i> Now Abraham says two things: firstly, regarding the Lord’s closing statement with which He had left Abraham, and which being so important was therefore concise,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">16. Later in the <i>Gevia‘ Kesef</i>, fol. 152a, Kaspi states that the importance and profundity of Scriptural description, vary inversely with the length of any given passage, so that the shorter the description, the more important it is.</i> Abraham says, “What wilt thou give me?” This is in reference to any future rewards that might be bestowed. (Secondly), regarding the Lord’s opening words, “I am thy shield,” which is in reference to what is already in Abraham’s possession, Abraham says, “That which you protect can only benefit Eliezer, who, because I have no son, is necessarily my (only) inheritor.” Subsequently, having stated the Lord’s words and their explanation, the giver of the Torah records that Abraham believed in the Lord, “and He counted it to him for righteousness.”<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">17. V. 6.</i>
Then the giver of the Torah wrote that the Lord, in speaking with Abraham again, promised that he would inherit this land, the land of Canaan. While this had already been promised, (it is repeated here) for the promise to Abraham was to be repeated twice. (This happened) the first time in “unto thy seed will I give this land,”<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">18. Gen. 12:17.</i> and the second time in “to thee will I give it and to thy seed forever.”<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">19. Gen. 13:15. Both these verses state that the land will be given to Abraham (“thee”), but in fact the land was possessed only by his descendants. Kaspi now explains the answer.</i> This teaches us of three modes of expression, for on occasion Hebrew will refer to “a man” and intend his seed. This is because it is correct to associate a father with what will happen to his seed, and vice versa. This is something precious in the prophetic books,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">20. In <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i> (MK II 39) he says, “one of the secrets of the Torah is that it usually associates the father with what is intended for the son, and vice versa.”</i> (such as) in the words of the Lord to Elijah<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">21. I Kings 21:29, where Elijah foretells the fall of Ahab, only to defer the decree till the period of Ahab’s descendants. This is consistent with Kaspi’s position that no divine decree can be rescinded, it can only be understood in a new light. See <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i> (MK II 286 ff.).</i> when Ahab’s fate was foretold (by God). Remember this.
In this context the giver of the Torah taught us another precious lesson. This is (occasioned by the fact) that even the initial promise, that which promised the bestowal (of children),<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">22. The word <i>zera‘</i> is found in the margin of the MS.</i> had already been promised several times by the Lord. It had been written first as “unto thy seed will I give all this land,”<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">23. Gen. 12:7.</i> and more specifically the second time in “I will make thy seed as the dust of the earth, so that if a man can number the dust of the earth, then shall thy seed also be numbered. Arise walk through the land …”<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">24 Gen. 13:16–17.</i> Accordingly, these promises having already been made, (God’s) words here are superfluous and repetitious. Abraham’s words present other problems, for in regard to the promise of the land, the Torah says, “And he believed in the Lord,” yet on the two previous occasions this is not stated. Did he not | believe until the third time?<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">25. The question is raised by other exegetes. Nachmanides had answered by quoting Jer. 18:9–10, where the prophetic decree of blessing is changed to punishment. It is interesting that Kaspi quotes these very vv. in <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i> (MK II 287) to prove his point against Maimonides’ insistence that only decrees of punishment are subject to change, yet here Kaspi does not explain Abraham’s behavior as motivated by a fear that he might have misunderstood God’s initial promise.</i> (This is even more questionable) for he did already have faith, proof being that after each vision it states, “And he builded there an altar unto the Lord.”<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">26. Gen. 12:7, 13:18.</i> (The answer to all these questions) is that this is all to teach us that it is proper for a king to make promises to his servants many times in various ways, on some occasions the servant will remain silent, while at other times he will answer and question. This is certainly the case when the king’s words incorporate words and names that are equivocal.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">27. In <i>’Adnei Kesef</i> (AK II 51) he says, “words and decrees are given by God to the prophet, and sometimes they are not understood at all, or understood only in part …”</i> In this instance “seed” and “son” are equivocal terms, for one can correctly refer thereby either to a blood-relative or to one who is merely nurtured by (an adoptive) father, as is the case with “and was unto him as a daughter.”<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">28. II Sam. 12:3. In the MS <i>le-vat</i> instead of <i>ke-vat</i>. Kimḥi makes a similar point when he says that “seed” could refer to one of the members of his family, as in the verse “speaking peace to all his seed” (Esther 10:3).</i> Because all this is imaginable, and is similar to what is mentioned in the <i>Metaphysics</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">29. The passage in question occurs in the <i>Metaphysics</i> V, 15, fol. 1021a: “For it is in this way that the father is called the father of his son; for the one has acted and the other has been acted upon in a certain way.” See above nn. 20–21.</i> Abraham therefore inquired on this third occasion if (God’s) words intended the (servant) living in his house. God then explained to him that “he that shall come forth out of thine own bowels (shall be thine heir).” Thus, God’s words having been reiterated several times, Abraham trusted and believed even more.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">30. Kimḥi explained what these several times were: first God said, “Out of thine own bowels”; then he is taken outside to see the stars as a sign that this will come true; finally God mentions His own name as if to swear by it.</i>
Because (the promise of seed) was destined to be fulfilled in his own lifetime, and was not fraught with dangers, Abraham did not request a sign as he had done regarding the promise of entry into the land, which was in the distant future and accompanied by pronounced danger.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">31. Gersonides gives as the reason for this request: “for he did not know if his descendants would merit the necessary divine providence.” Here once again (cf. above n. 25) Kaspi chooses not to press this point against Maimonides.</i> Furthermore (he did not continue to ask for signs) for the longer a sage and a prophet continue to have recourse to the intellect, they become more and more casuistic. Here, (further) request for signs would have been casuistic, shrewd, and devious, even had that request been for a proof that was speculative or prophetic.

Chapter 8

The elect and most wise sages, may their memory be blessed, said that “Moses wrote his book and the Book of Job.”<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">1. BT <i>Bava’ Batra’</i> 14b.</i> Elsewhere they said that “Job never existed, for he was but a parable.”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">2. Ibid. 15a. A casual comparison might lead one to conclude that neither Job nor the patriarchs existed. See above Chapter 1 regarding allegorical exegesis.</i> Their intention was to say that Moses composed the Book of Job, in his wisdom, to inform us of great truths,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">3. In his commentary to the Book of Job (AS I 137), Kaspi takes issue with Maimonides (<i>Guide</i> III:22–23) for leaving the question of Job’s existence unresolved. Kaspi insists on the literal comprehension of “There was a man in the land of Uz, whose name was Job” (Job 1:1), for “if we doubt the existence of Job … we could doubt the existence of all the personalities in the Bible … and if some of our ancestors did not know his time and place, that is not important, for I believe that he existed as recorded …”</i> similar to what is said of the earliest philosophers. Such is the work written by Ibn Zur‘a that tells the parable of the argument of man with the rest of the animals.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">4. This tenth-century translator of Aristotle’s <i>Historia Animalium</i> used logic and philosophy in studying the Bible, like Kaspi. Cf. <i>Encyclopedia of Islam</i>, s.v. “Ibn Zur‘a.” His writings are mostly lost.</i> When a story is told as if it actually happened, one can learn from the dialogue several matters in practical philosophy, as well as precious things regarding natural science, whose goal is the attainment of the perfection and immortality of the soul. This is true of the Book of Job in every respect.
There is no doubt | that the first man existed, as did Noah, the patriarchs, and the (twelve) tribes. God forbid that the (sages), in attributing both (the Torah and Job) to Moses, meant thereby that the Torah is similar to Job in this respect. Their intention, rather, was to state that the Torah, like (Job), contains fundamental aspects of practical and theoretical philosophy. (Their statement) also teaches us that in the Torah some places contain parables and metaphors, including the episode (in the life) of Abraham regarding the heifer and the she-goat and ram, as well as that entire parable, and the incident of Jacob’s ladder,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">5. Gen. 28:12.</i> etc. This is especially true of this book (of Genesis) in the Torah, for in my opinion it is this book which the sages referred to as “the work of Creation.”<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">6. BT <i>Megillah</i> 25a, <i>Genesis Rabbah</i> 1.</i> The most profound part of (Genesis) is the section concluding, “But Noah found grace in the eyes of the Lord.”<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">7. Gen. 6:8. He means that the “work of Creation” refers essentially to the chapters preceding the flood in Gen. 6. On the importance that Kaspi attaches to the various divisions of the Scriptures, see above Chapter 2.</i> The book (of Genesis), containing in its entirety all that transpired before the birth of Moses, is the most important book in the Torah. This fact (of it having taken place prior to the birth of Moses) constitutes one of the special aspects of Genesis, for the Book of Exodus, and the following books, were easy (for Moses) to write, having seen everything with his own eyes. It was difficult, however, (to write) of what had occurred thousands of years earlier. Nevertheless, for Moses it was “but a small thing,”<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">8. BT <i>Berakhot</i> 33b.</i> for nothing in the past was beyond Moses’ ability to write, even that which occured thousands of years previously. (It was no more difficult) than writing of what was to occur in years to come, as I will explain elsewhere. As a rule, whatever Moses wrote regarding past and future (events), he knew and wrote in accordance with the word (of God), for everything was conveyed to him from the Lord, or if you will, from heaven.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">9. Kaspi insists that all of the Torah is from heaven, including all of Deuteronomy, “as those of us who philosophize know” (MK I 110). The final verses of Deuteronomy were likewise included, for “those things that relate to future events are no different from (future predictions in) the rest of the Torah” (MK II 310).</i> This is especially true of those stories in Genesis referring to the past, for they are all categorical, i.e., anecdotal,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">10. In <i>Ṭirat Kesef</i> (MK I 1) Kaspi divides the Torah into two kinds of statements: (a) <i>’Omer gozer</i> (narrative), (b) <i>’Eino gozer</i> (prescriptive). Only the former affirms some truth, for a command does not state any facts. He discusses this further in the <i>Ṣeror Ha-Kesef</i> (fol. 231b). Kaspi’s position here is similar to that of Alfarabi in <i>Five Chapters of Logic</i> (MS Vatican 49, fol. 106a), stating: “the philosophical narrative is that which is called demonstrative, and its purpose is to teach the truth and to cause to understand …” This is quoted in Mesch, <i>Studies</i>, p. 86. See also H. Blumberg, “Alfarabi’s Five Chapters on Logic,” <i>PAAJR</i> VI (1934), pp. 119–121.</i> statements. This is true of many preceding matters. Even though the sages said, “the (entire) Torah given at Sinai was written in black fire upon white fire,”<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">11. JT <i>Shekalim</i> 6:1, <i>Canticles Rabbah</i> 5:9. The statement does not distinguish between different books of the Torah.</i> my opinion is that they were referring primarily to Genesis, for it is the most important (of all), being “apples of gold in settings of silver.”<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">12. Prov. 25:11, which is quoted in the Introduction to the <i>Guide</i> in reference to the exoteric and underlying esoteric levels of Scripture.</i> | This (last verse) is especially true of Genesis, as it contains the exalted secrets and mysteries of the Torah, as well as the commandments of the heart<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">13. In Hebrew <i>miṣvot libbiyot</i>. Kaspi divides the commandments into three classes (of the heart, of action, of the tongue). All are necessary, yet the first of these is the most important. As he puts it, “were we angels we would need only the commandments of the intellect [i.e., of the heart] (MK I 6). This classification follows Ibn Ezra’s <i>Yesod Mora’</i> (Prague, 1833), pp. 27–32, which is referred to in <i>‘Ammudei Kesef</i> (AM 134). Cf. Mesch, <i>Studies</i>, pp. 84 ff.</i> that subsequently occur in the Torah in the form of nonanecdotal statements,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">14. He is referring to commandments, which are as a class prescriptive, as opposed to the narrative statements which are categorical.</i> such as “I am the Lord thy God,”<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">15. Exod. 20:2.</i> “thou shalt have no other Gods,”<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">16. Exod. 20:3.</i> and “Hear, O Israel, the Lord our God, the Lord is one.”<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">17. Deut. 6:4.</i> (This is also true of) “thou shalt love …”<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">18. Deut. 6:5.</i> and “thou shalt fear.”<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">19. Lev. 19:14, 32; 25:17, 36, 43.</i> All these (commandments) come in the book (of Genesis) in narrative and anecdotal form.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">20. Cf. above n. 10. Maimonides also explains that Scriptural narrative is a necessary utility for the law, when he says (<i>Guide</i> III:50): “Either they give a correct notion of an opinion that is a pillar of the law, or they rectify some action so that wrongdoing and aggression should not occur between men.”</i> For this reason the sages praised this book by referring to it as “the work of Creation,” as stated above.

Chapter 9

The wise know that a term when used as a verb can refer equally to a distant and a proximate agent, and that these adjectives (distant, proximate) can be understood in varying degrees, both more and less.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">1. This refers to Gen. 15:7, “I am the Lord that brought thee out from Ur Kasdim …” which is here interpreted as referring to God acting through intermediate causes. This is similar to Maimonides’ statement in the <i>Guide</i> II:48 affirming that actions are attributed directly to God, even when His will is realized through a natural framework of causes.</i> Therefore in saying “act, do, make, create, move, bring forth, write, speak, say, build, destroy, forbid, permit, give, leave, eat, see, hear, remember,” as well as every verb used in any expression, it is correct, necessary, and proper to affirm, absolutely and without qualification, that (such verbs) can refer equally to the nearest agent and the furthest. In the Torah this (affirmation) has reference to the Lord, for He is above all the Separate Intelligences, the Heavenly Spheres, and so much more above earthly matter.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">2. On these worlds, and the divine role, see above chap. I.</i> (This is the meaning of the verse) “And He made them wander to and fro in the wilderness forty years,”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">3. Num. 32:13.</i> meaning that the Lord on high caused the Israelites to move, but (in such a way that) He was necessarily only the distant, prime mover, while Moses was the most proximate external (cause). To this one can add “writes,” “says,” and “speaks” (when attributed to God in the Torah) as well as the other verbs that I may or may not have mentioned.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">4. In <i>Ṭirat Kesef</i> (MK I 65) Kaspi writes, “the agent and the one acted on join together, so in several ways the agent is considered as acted upon.” He quotes, “and Joseph made ready his chariot” (Gen. 46:29), where Joseph only issued the command that this be done. So too is “So Solomon built the house” (I Kings 6:14).</i>
Regarding that which Moses wrote in the Torah, that “I am the Lord that brought thee out from Ur Kasdim, to give thee this land to inherit it: And he said, ‘O Lord, whereby shall I know that I shall inherit it?’”<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">5. Gen. 15:7–8.</i> (we learn that) from these words God promised Abraham that in the future the land (of Canaan) would be completely inherited by his descendants, as was realized by Joshua. Abraham then asked for a sign and proof of this (promise), for | his words “whereby shall I know” are no different from those attributed by the writer of the Book of Kings to Hezekiah when he said, “What shall be the sign that the Lord will heal me?”<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">6. II Kings 20:8. In <i>’Adnei Kesef</i> (AK I 73) the reader is referred to the <i>Gevia’ Kesef</i>, in explaining that Hezekiah, like Abraham here, did not request proof because he doubted God.</i> It was also no worse than that written in the Torah that the Lord on his own accord informed Moses, “This shall be the token unto thee that I have sent thee.”<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">7. Exod. 3:12.</i> As a rule, a prophet or a sage may at times request a sign or proof, while at other times he may rely in the matter on other things. Sometimes the teacher may provide (the sign) of his own accord, or from a recognition that his student doubts his words to a greater or lesser degree. Either he will provide rational proof or else (he will do it in such a way) that others will not doubt. This is similar to Aristotle’s mentioning in his book signs and proofs of the continued existence of the heavens, for the benefit of his students.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">8. Aristotle, <i>De Caelo</i>, I, 10–12, which proves that the heaven is necessarily ungenerated and indestructible.</i> There are others like this, as Maimonides wrote in (the <i>Guide</i>) I:21.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">9. I have not located such a reference in that chapter of the <i>Guide</i>.</i>
While we cannot compare or equate the Torah to other books, nor can we compare Moses, who wrote the Torah, with other authors, (nor can we compare them to) the Lord, who was the prime writer and giver of the Torah,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">10. This establishes the exact referent for the less precise phrasings that are found elsewhere in the <i>Gevia’ Kesef</i>, such as “the writer of the Torah” and “the bestower of the Torah.” See also AK I 136.</i> nonetheless we may conceive of a relation between this holy book and its meaning, and other books, by way of analogy.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">11. Cf. below chap. X, n. 16.</i> This is similar, for example, to our describing Natural Matter,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">12. In Heb. <i>hiyyuli ha-tiv‘i</i>, which in Aristotelian metaphysics is the cosmic First Matter, from which all matter is derived.</i> which is the work of God, by analogy to the lowly product, i.e., a container of urine.
For this reason, (we can explain) that when Abraham the student (of God) asked for a sign and proof from his great teacher, the Lord, that He should verify in any way what had been asserted, it was just as Alexander might have asked of his teacher, Aristotle,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">13. In 343 <small>B.C.E. </small>Aristotle was invited to Pella by Philip of Macedon, to undertake the education of his son Alexander, who was then thirteen years old. When Alexander (later to be known as Alexander the Great) ascended the throne in 336, Aristotle returned to his native city. See F. Copleston, <i>A History of Philosophy</i>, vol. I, pt. II (Garden City: Image Books, 1962), pp. 10–11.</i> or ’Abaye of Rava’ his teacher.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">14. The MS has <i>Rava</i> ’, but ’Abbaye’s teacher was in fact Rabbah, according to Rashi’s comments on BT <i>Shabbat</i> 5b, s.v. <i>mi-de’amar</i>.</i> Everything is according to the occupation and level of the individual, for “the fool will believe everything, while the wise will understand well,”<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">15. Prov. 14:5.</i> or in any case (the wise) will want to know the meaning to the greatest possible extent. Such is the nature of the questions that sages ask of the Lord, and that the prophets ask and are praised for. It is through this that “The Lord rejoices in his works.”<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">16. Ps. 104:31.</i> Thus when Abraham said, | “Shall a child be born unto him that is a hundred years old?,”<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">17. Gen. 17:17.</i> it was the same as Moses’ saying, “If flocks and herds be slain, will they suffice them?”<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">18. Num. 11:22.</i> There are many passages like this.
In answer, the Lord tells him, “Take me a heifer of three years old” until “And Abram drove them away,” and then, “It came to pass that, when the sun was going down, a deep sleep fell upon Abram, and He said unto Abram, ‘… for the iniquity of the Amorite is not yet full.’ … and it came to pass when the sun went down …”<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">19. Gen. 15:9–17.</i> One should know that occasionally it is the custom of the Lord to inform the prophets, his students, of future things that will come to pass, using phrases and words that occur in the form of parable and metaphor, as was done in the case of Jacob’s parable of the ladder with its appropriate (symbols).<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">20. Gen. 28:12.</i> It was similar in the case of Ezekiel, when all of Metaphysics occurred in a parable;<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">21. Ezek. 1.</i> in the case of Jeremiah with regard to the future, in the parables that begin, “I see the rod of an almond tree” and “the seething pot”;<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">22. Jer. 1:11, 13.</i> and in the case of Amos, in the parables that begin “Plumbline” and a “basket of summer fruit.”<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">23. Amos 7:7, 8:1. These parables are discussed in the <i>Guide</i> II:43.</i> In these cases it is not necessary that the words always come in one form, for it is in accordance with a teacher’s wisdom that he makes changes in his words, while retaining the original meaning. What is meant by this is shown when on occasion the Lord inquires of a prophet, “What do you see,” and the prophet answers, “I see thus and so,” to which the Lord replies, “The explanation is thus and so.” This is what occurred in the cases of Jeremiah and Amos, mentioned above. Sometimes not all the details of the question and answer are mentioned, as when the Lord merely provides the parable for the “student,” who later establishes his own explanation.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">24. This assertion is consistent with Kaspi’s doctrine that many divine decrees are only understood in the light of subsequent events, avoiding the need to postulate changes in divine decrees. See <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i> (MK II 289).</i> This happened to Ezekiel, when the Lord provided him with a parable,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">25. Ezek. 37.</i> in which, according to the most outstanding of our sages, “the dead in Ezekiel were a parable.”<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">26. BT <i>Sanhedrin</i> 92b.</i> And even though the Lord subsequently provided him with an explanation, saying, “These bones are the whole house of Israel, and I will put my spirit in you, and ye shall live,”<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">27. Ezek. 37:11, 14. In <i>‘Ammudei Kesef</i> (AM 8) Kaspi says that even though this chapter occurred in a vision, as a parable, there is no doubt that “the resurrection of the dead is true.”</i> it was not necessary for Him to say to Ezekiel, “Know that these bones are the whole house of Israel,” for had He omitted this, the meaning would have been retained, and the omission would have been correct.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">28. In <i>Ṭirat Kesef</i> (MK I 27) Kaspi says similarly, “we will not give a reason for each word [of a Scriptural parable] in every place. Instead we will give a reason in general …” Kaspi moulded his commentaries on Scriptural use of parable, sometimes explaining at length, at other times in brief.</i>
Accordingly, as Maimonides said,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">29. Introduction to the <i>Guide</i>.</i> it is not always necessary that all the details of a parable | and its interpretation be identical. Here (in the vision of the Sundering) the parable includes everything from the term “heifer” until the words “and Abram drove them away.”<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">30. V. 11.</i> The interpretation extends from “thy seed shall be a stranger” until “is not yet full.”<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">31. Vv. 13–16.</i> Accordingly, while the solution covers four verses, it is found in only two verses: i.e., “Know of a surety” and “In the fourth generation.” It excludes “for the iniquity of the Amorite is not yet full,” for these two other verses do not explain what is in the parable, but rather they provide new information that together with the others relates to what was intended for Abraham. Now the Lord, (like) a teacher, was careful to conceal in the middle that which was not part of the main explanation of the parable, as is proper for one who wishes to conceal and hide,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">32. The MS here has <i>le-ha’amin</i>, meaning “to foster trust,” a rather unusual form of the verb in the present context. More appropriate would be a reading <i>le-haṭmin</i>, lit. “to hide.”</i> as I have explained in regard to Jacob’s words “as long as men come to Shiloh.”<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">33. Gen. 49:10. This refers to a passage in the <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i> (MK II 105) where he rebuts the Christological interpretation that claims that the word <i>shiloh</i> is a reference to Jesus. At one point there he states that these four letters (<i>shiloh</i>) “are hidden and intermingled, so that no one should notice them unless he has very keen sight …”; i.e., the term is included to confuse and mislead, so as to hide the true meaning of the verse. <br>Leo Strauss, in an essay on the methods that Maimonides utilized in the <i>Guide</i> to conceal and reveal the truth, enumerates this very same point as a primary vehicle for such a literary feat. See L. Strauss, “The Literary Character of the <i>Guide for the Perplexed,” Persecution and the Art of Writing</i> (Chicago: University of Chicago Press, 1952), p. 52.</i> Likewise there are other words in the parable that are not an integral part of the intended promise, including “and he laid each half over against the other,”<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">34. V. 10.</i> as I will explain later. It appears from all of this that the Lord wanted to reveal to Abraham that some of his seed would be slaves in a strange land not owned by them, but He did not want to reveal to him which land (that would be), except to tell him that the fourth generation would escape from that servitude. This is what occurred in Egypt, as it is written, “And Joseph died, and all his brethren, and all that generation,” and then, “Now there arose a new king over Egypt,”<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">35. Exod. 1:6.</i> which began the enslavement of the tribes. Included were the generations of Kohath, Amram, and Moses.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">36. Rashi refers to the four generations as Judah, Pereẓ, Ḥeẓron, and Caleb. Nachmanides understands the reference to be to four generations of Amorites. Kaspi here follows Kimḥi’s listing of the generations. For further discussion of this, see C. Gordon, “Hebrew Origins in the Light of Recent Discovery,” in <i>Biblical and Other Studies</i>, ed. A. Altmann (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 1963), p. 4.</i> Thus, the words “thy seed shall be a stranger in a land that is not theirs and shall serve them; and they shall afflict them” accord with the manner of every wise teacher, and are certainly appropriate words for the great teacher who is the Lord.
“Thy seed shall be a stranger” refers to Egypt, saying that his seed would serve and be afflicted by the Egyptians.
“Four hundred years” has no allusion in the parable, but it was considered correct by the teacher to reveal the complete explanation and intended meaning at that point. This figure is related to “thy seed shall be a stranger,” but the phrase “and they shall serve them and they shall afflict them” is not necessarily part of the figure (of four hundred years). In a sense it would be correct | to say that they were afflicted for (four hundred years) during all that time, for it is the lot of every stranger (to be afflicted).<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">37. This is an attempt by Kaspi to answer the problem of the actual duration of the stay in Egypt, which would appear to have lasted less than the four hundred years foretold in Gen. 15:13. Gersonides considered the four-hundred-year period to have begun with Jacob’s birth; Kaspi follows Rashi, who computes the period from the birth of Isaac, and follows Kimḥi, who had stated that “affliction” could also refer to a condition of alienation, such as that which Isaac and Jacob experienced.</i> Reliable evidence of this (affliction) is provided by the (names) of the wells dug by Isaac, called <i>‘Eseḳ</i> (“contention”) and <i>Siṭnah</i> (“enmity”),<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">38. Gen. 26:20–21. Both names reflect the alienation of Isaac.</i> as well as the incident of (the rape of) Dinah in Jacob’s lifetime.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">39. Gen. 34. This reflected the abuse and alienation of Jacob.</i> In any case, Isaac and Jacob were aliens, downtrodden and subservient for a time in Egypt,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">40. Actually Isaac never lived in Egypt. One explanation might be that Kaspi alludes to Isaac’s sojourn in the land of the Philistines, to the south of the land of Canaan, in Gen. 26.</i> a fact which justifies the figure of four hundred years, and removes any inconsistency. However, part of (the four hundred years) was more intense and literal as far as the words “serve” and “afflict.” (This is consistent) with what I mentioned above, saying that descriptive words can be used in an exact literal sense, and also in a loose sense.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">41. See below.</i> The Lord revealed to Abraham that the words “thy seed shall be a stranger” referred to three generations, when He stated afterwards “the fourth generation …” That fourth generation was the one that entered the land in the time of Joshua, the generation that was led about in the desert having been the third generation that endured in part the suffering in Egypt, starting on the day that Moses was born and continuing until the time of the burning bush, when he was eighty years old.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">42. Exod. 3:2. Kaspi computes this age by the fact that at his death Moses was 120 years old, which was after 40 years in the desert; therefore Moses was 80 when he appeared at Mount Sinai the first time.</i> (In this matter) it is not necessary to detail the components and the people involved.
The explanation of the parable (of the Sundering) is that the three animals that are mentioned<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">43. In Heb. <i>shilush</i>, lit. “triplicate.”</i> are a metaphor for the three generations discussed above, while the turtle-dove and the young pigeon refer to the fourth generation. The Lord commanded him to sunder the three species so that their blood could be offered to the Lord. The words “all these” (“he took him …”) refers only to the livestock, in that later it is written, “but the birds divided he not.” The word “all” poses no problem (by implying that Abraham took everything) to one who knows the holy language.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">44. In the <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i> (MK II 162) Kaspi says, “the word <i>kol</i> [all] in Hebrew and its equivalent in Provençal are used when referring to the major portion of anything, just as long as a large number is involved …”</i> All of this refers to the beating and pursuing of the Israelites that took place during those three generations,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">45. In the <i>Ṭirat Kesef</i> (MK I 81) they are described as “tortured and dissipated.”</i> which resulted in their being considered corpses. One need not say “like corpses,” for they really were dead bodies, in accordance with what is explained in the <i>Metaphysics</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">46. This is in reference to the <i>Metaphysics</i> V, 16, 1021b, where Aristotle says that “we say a thing has been completely spoilt and completely destroyed when it in no way falls short of destruction and badness, but is at its last point. That is why death too is by a figure of speech called the end, because both are last things.”</i> and certainly in view of what is written in the <i>Posterior Analytics</i><sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">47. The meaning of likeness is not discussed in the <i>Posterior Analytics</i>, but in the <i>Metaphysics</i> X, 3, 1054b, the following is found: “things are alike, if, not being absolutely the same, nor without difference in respect of their concrete substance, they are the same in form … other things are alike, if having the form, and being things in which difference of degree is possible, they have no difference of degree.”</i> and the <i>De Caelo</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">48. In <i>De Caelo</i>, III, 6, 305a, the following is found: “a destructive process which succeeds in destroying, i.e., resolving into smaller bodies.”</i>
The fact that the birds were not sundered, i.e., the turtle-dove and the young pigeon, was in reference to the absence of persecution | of the fourth generation at the hands of the Egyptians.
The meaning of the birds of prey that descended, which included birds that stamp on their prey, as is known from Jeremiah’s words,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">49. Jer. 22:3.</i> as well as those of Ezekiel<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">50. Ezek. 22:29.</i> in explaining [“strangers” as a description of the three kinds of deceased],<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">51. The words in the brackets present a problem, in that they do not appear to belong here, if indeed they refer to “birds of prey” as a generic term. An alternative explanation of these words is that the references to Jeremiah and Ezekiel are to Jer. 12:9 and Ezek. 39:4, both of which use the phrase generically (cf. <i>’Adnei Kesef</i> AK II 8). But the problem with this is that if so, the words included in the brackets remain unexplained. Kimḥi, in his commentary to this verse, also includes the “birds of prey” as a generic term that refers to birds that stamp.</i> is in reference to the coming together of all the Egyptians, king and people, for the purpose of enslaving them. As it is written, “and he said unto his people,”<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">52. Exod. 1:9.</i> and “the heart of Pharaoh and his servants were turned.”<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">53. Exod. 14:5.</i>
When Abraham drove away the stamping birds of prey that drink blood,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">54. V. 11.</i> it was in reference to the three generations that were not completely destroyed, for (after all) six hundred thousand warriors, not counting women and children, escaped from Egypt.
The Lord, as a teacher would do, described three species of animal to Abraham—a heifer, a she-goat, and a ram. These were, as noted earlier, a parable and metaphor for the three generations of Jews who were strangers and servants. The number is appropriate: three species that correspond to three generations. The number “three” is a properly equivocal term, for the intent is to one she- goat, one heifer, and one ram, a sum of three, with each one constituting a third, no matter which one is placed first, which one second, and which one third.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">55. I have not found a precedent for this explanation. Rashi, Nachmanides, and Kimḥi all explain that there were three animals of each type. Nachmanides, however, is aware that according to that explanation, the term <i>sheloshah</i> would have been preferable to the uncommon <i>meshulash</i>. Ibn Ezra and Gersonides explain that the animals were to be three years old. Kaspi elsewhere (MK I 80, MK II 52) is unclear but it would appear that there he follows Rashi. Here, however, he makes it clear that there was one animal of each type, so that each was one-third of the total.</i> Now (the Lord) as the teacher, mentioned these three animals three times, to emphasize and reinforce that this was indeed coming from God.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">56. Later (chap. XIX) Kaspi enlarges upon this theme.</i> Joseph learnt from this (repetition) when he said in reference to the cows and the heaped stalks, “The dream of Pharaoh is one … and for that the dream was doubled twice, it is because the thing is established by God,”<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">57. Gen. 41:25, 32.</i> all the more had it been repeated thrice, for “the threefold cord is not quickly broken,”<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">58. Eccles. 4:12.</i> and “a ruin, a ruin, a ruin, will I make it.”<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">59. Ezek. 21:32.</i> Were one to say there were actually three heifers and three she-goats and three rams,<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">60. This refers to Rashi, Nachmanides, and Kimḥi; cf. n. 55.</i> that too would be correct, for the truth is | the truth no matter which side it is looked upon. As Aristotle said, “the truth is its own witness and consistent on every side.”<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">61. Cf. chap. V, n. 1.</i>
I can now explain the meaning of the words <i>‘eglah</i> (“heifer”), <i>‘ez</i> (“she-goat”), and <i>’ayil</i> (“ram”) in accordance with their precise significance. (This is possible) as these things are unlike sacrifices, of which Maimonides said that it is impossible for them to be without some species or number.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">62. <i>Guide</i> III:26.</i> The Torah describes the congregation of Israel first as a heifer (<i>‘eglah</i>), which is customary, as it is written, “Ephraim is a heifer well-broken.”<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">63. Hos. 10:11.</i> Furthermore, <i>‘eglah</i> is derived from <i>‘agol</i> (“circle”), as in a continuously revolving cycle that alludes to (that which repeats itself) in our patriarchs and ourselves.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">64. This alludes to the rabbinic dictum that “the deeds of the fathers are a sign for their descendants” (BT <i>Soṭah</i> 34a).</i> A meaning is also found through metathesis<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">65. Kaspi’s use of metathesis in the understanding of Biblical terms has been discussed at length above in Chapter 2. Kaspi considers these <i>mehupakhim</i> to be an important tool in exegesis, one that presupposes an intimate knowledge of the Hebrew language (cf. <i>Menorat Kesef</i>, AS II 108). Kaspi’s main source for this method is Ibn Janaḥ’s <i>Sefer Ha-Riḳmah</i>, ed. M. Wilensky (Jerusalem: Academy for the Hebrew Language, 1964), vol. I, p. 362, and ed. note, tracing this usage to Saadya.</i> in a manner that is similar to Maimonides’ allusion regarding <i>regel-‘egel</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">66. <i>Guide</i> II:43.</i> Accordingly, (the metathesis of <i>‘egel</i>) is <i>ge‘olah</i> (“loathing”), as in “thy soul loathed,”<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">67. Jer. 14:9.</i> similar to what Maimonides noted in connection with <i>ḥovel</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">68. <i>Guide</i> II:43.</i> a verb whose metathesis is <i>vohel</i>, alluding to “and their soul also loathed me.”<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">69. Zech. 11:8.</i> (<i>‘Egel</i> thus) refers to the fact that the (Israelites in Egypt) would be loathsome in their servitude.
<i>‘Ez</i> (“she-goat”) alludes to <i>‘oz</i> (“strength”), for it would require much fortitude to endure the suffering in Egypt, (and necessitate their turning to) Him who “giveth power to the faint.”<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">70. Isa. 40:29.</i> In addition (there is a reference) by way of metathesis in that <i>za‘</i> (“move”) implies that that generation would wander in that place.
<i>’Ayil</i> (“ram”)<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">71. V. 9.</i> is (in reference to) the verse “as a man that has no help (<i>’ayil</i>),”<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">72. Ps. 88:5.</i> which gives it the meaning of “strength,” as with <i>‘oz</i>. The metathesis (of <i>’ayil</i>) is <i>ya’al</i>, which gives the meaning of one who desires other circumstances,<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">73. See below in chap. XIV where this is discussed further.</i> for the will of the Lord was (otherwise), as in “he bowed his shoulder to bear (that which was not his will).”<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">74. Gen. 49:15.</i>
The whole parable relates their (destiny) to animals, for those people were as animals, not having received the true Torah, which “maketh wise the simple.”<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">75. Ps. 19:18.</i> Furthermore, the (Israelites) were like weak animals in relation to their oppressors, and for this reason a heifer, and not a cow, is mentioned, for a cow implies that which is strong, as in “a stubborn cow.”<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">76. Hos. 4:16.</i>
The turtle-dove and pigeon are birds that refer to the fourth generation, which would escape from there like a winged bird<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">77. In commenting on Isa. 28:5, Kaspi derives <i>ṣippor</i> (“bird”) from “rapid movement” (AK I 128).</i> to go to Israel, as explained above. In addition | the word <i>tor</i> is a homonym, indicating a specified period, as in “when the turn (<i>tor</i>) of Esther was come.”<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">78. Esther 2:15.</i> This is true both of the agent and the one acted upon,<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">79. This is repeated subsequently on fol. 149.</i> for from the point of view of Israel the sages said in <i>Hallel</i>, “from here we learn that God calculates the redemption,”<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">80. This is a reference to the Passover Haggadah, in the section beginning “Blessed be He who keeps His promise to Israel,” which quotes vv. 13–14.</i> which I explained in the context of the burning bush;<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">81. <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i> (MK II 127) explains the Exodus as being necessary (a) to redeem the Israelites, and (b) to punish the Egyptians.</i> from the point of view (of the acted upon), it is said of the Amorites and their neighbors, “for the iniquity of the Amorite is not yet full.”<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">82. V. 16.</i> The words of Jeremiah regarding Nebuchadnezzar, “until his time come,”<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">83. Jer. 27:7.</i> are similar to this. (A similar specified period is found) in Ezekiel’s statement foretelling the destruction of the land of Israel and the victory of Nebuchadnezzar, “A ruin, a ruin, a ruin, shall I make it; this also shall come no more until he come whose right it is, and I will give it him.”<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">84. Ezek. 21:32. He means here that while the Israelites deserved to have their land ruined, Nebuchadnezzar also had a right to possess it.</i>
<i>Gozal</i> (“pigeon”) is likewise equivocal, in that it refers also to stealing (<i>gazal</i>). This term provides a complete description (of the generation entering the land) that is consistent with “they are a nation void of counsel.”<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">85. Deut. 32:28.</i> The generation that laid siege to the land of Israel was compared to a pigeon (<i>gozal</i>) in its relation to the Canaanites,<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">86. In <i>Ṭirat Kesef</i> (MK I 75) Kaspi links the morality of Israel’s possession of the land to the principle that “Israel is not governed by the Zodiac” (BT <i>Nedarim</i> 32a), whereby there is a direct divine participation in the issue of when the Israelites are to take possession or be evicted from the land. On the question of natural laws and the fortunes of the Jews, see Pines, “Histabberut,” pp. 298 ff.</i> in accordance with the opinion of many, or (at least) some,<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">87. In <i>Ṭ</i> irat <i>Kesef</i> (MR I 121) Kaspi refers to “many of our poeple” who object to Jews feeling free to take the land of Israel and consider it their own.</i> including Joshua himself, who wrote, “And the men pursued after them.”<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">88. Josh. 2:7. This verse implies that the land was taken with stealth and deceit.</i> Reliable evidence of this is that in Deuteronomy Moses compares the taking of the land of Canaan by the Israelites to the taking of the Horite land by the descendants of Esau, and the land of the Rephaim by the Ammonites.<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">89. Deut. 2:16–24.</i> The (operative) principle is “the Lord gave and the Lord hath taken away,”<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">90. Job 1:21.</i> or as Jeremiah said in the name of the Lord, “I have made the earth”<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">91. Jer. 27:5. cf. below chap. XXIII, p. 275, in the context of the slaughter of the Shechemites.</i> (and can give it to whomever I choose).
“And he laid each half over against the other”; this was only so that the flaming torch could pass between the sundered pieces so that the covenant could be made involving the Lord and Abram. By sundering these carcasses, (it was undertaken that the covenant) would not be sundered as they were.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">92. This symbolism is similar to that of sacrifices, in which the animal was offered so that the owner would be spared the same fate.</i> Such was the custom of old,<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">93. Gersonides explained the significance as being a demonstration that the parties to the covenant are as one body, on account of the love between them.</i> as it is written, “they cut the calf in twain and passed between the parts thereof.”<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">94. Jer. 34:18. The phrase “to cut [<i>likhrot</i>] a treaty” is derived from the practice of severing animals as the seal of a treaty. This custom is widely attested to in ancient times and exists among primitive peoples to this day; particularly among Hittites and Greeks there are many parallels. It is generally believed that when the contracting parties passed between the severed pieces, they accepted the covenant obligations and invoked upon themselves the fate of the animals if the terms of the pact were violated. See the commentary of Bechor Shor, who says (regarding Gen. 15:9) that the custom was to pass between the sundered parts of an animal. See also N. Sarna, <i>Understanding Genesis</i> (New York: Schocken, 1970), p. 126, and W. F. Albright, <i>Archaeology and the Religion of Israel</i> (Baltimore, 1942), p. 113.</i> In this context, | <i>‘agal</i> could be understood as alluding to <i>‘agalah</i> (“carriage”). If there are other (allusions), there would be no harm done.
“The pillar of fire” alludes to the glory of the Lord, for “the Lord thy God is a devouring fire,”<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">95. Deut. 4:24.</i> and the appearance of the Lord’s glory is that of a consuming fire.
“And also that nation whom they shall serve, will I judge, and afterward shall they come out with great substance.” Even though this is located in the explanation of the parable, it does not explain any words appearing in the parable, as I have stated above. Instead it contains a new teaching, unmentioned above. It is the same with “but thou shalt go to thy fathers in peace; thou shalt be buried in good old age,” which teaches that the promised destiny would not begin with himself, but with Isaac.
Notice another precise thing: throughout this vision there are three levels. Those coming later are more important than the earlier ones.<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">96. In <i>Ṭirat Kesef</i> (MK I 88) Kaspi writes, “as the prophetic seclusion continues, the illumination of knowledge and perfection increases in degree, similar to the moon in the first half of the month … as opposed to the second half of the month, which is to be compared to the knowledge of generally known things [<i>mefursamot</i>].” See also MK II 45, AK I 21, AS II 133.</i> This is correct when anyone secludes himself in examination of his soul, whether in a dimension of intellectual (endeavor) or prophetic (endeavor), as the philosophers have written.<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">97. This passage, and what follows it, reflects the following passage in the <i>Guide</i> II:41: “Prophetic revelation begins sometimes with a vision of prophecy. Thereupon the terror and the strong affection consequent upon the attainment of the perfection of the action of the imaginative faculty become intensified and then prophetic revelation comes, as is recounted of Abraham. For with regard to the beginning of that prophetic revelation, it is said: ‘The word of the Lord came unto Abram in a vision.’ And with regard to its termination: ‘And a deep sleep fell upon Abram,’ and so on. And after that: ‘And He said unto Abram,’ and so on.”</i> The first (stage) of this important event occurred (on the level of prophecy described by the phrase) “in a vision do I make myself known unto him.”<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">98. Num. 12:6.</i> That this stage took place at a specific hour during the day is proved by “when the sun was going down.”<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">99. He means that this stage occurred in the afternoon, when the sun was going down from its high point at noon.</i> When the day was coming to an end and the sun was about to set, Abram took strength a second time, and achieved a higher level. This is the meaning of “when the sun was going down.” The great seclusion caused: “a deep sleep fell upon Abram; and lo, a dread, even a great darkness, fell upon him.” At this level, greater than the preceding, the explanation of what had been hidden was achieved, i.e., the explanation of the (earlier) parable. This would happen with Jeremiah and Amos<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">100. Jer. 1; Amos 7:8.</i> in the wake of preceding (parables). Later Abraham took strength a third time, having preceded that with prophetic seclusion. (At that point) the sun had settled completely, so there was the intense darkness of night, even “thick darkness” (<i>‘alaṭah</i>), which was more intense than (mere) “darkness” (<i>ḥashekhah</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">101. V. 17.</i> In addition (at this level) there was a smoking furnace, as there was at Mount Sinai, as it is said, “and the smoke thereof ascended as the smoke of the furnace.”<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">102. Exod. 19:19.</i> At this point the Lord’s glory came down upon him in the image of a flaming torch, passing between these sundered parts, and by passing through (them) made a covenant with Abraham, as it is said, “On that day God made a covenant with Abram, saying,”<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">103. V. 18.</i> where (“on that day”) means “at that moment.” | This is like “on the day that thou eatest thereof”<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">104. Gen. 2:17, where “on that day” clearly means “at that moment.”</i> (meaning “at that moment that thou eatest thereof”).
The expression “to make a covenant,” as well as any expression of an oath (by the Lord), occurs in the sense of “the Torah speaks in the language of men.”<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">105. BT <i>Bava’ Meṣi‘a’</i> 31b, BT <i>Yevamot</i> 71a. See above Chapter 2.</i> It merely conveys the idea of something permanent and necessary. Now even though (the subject of the covenant) referred to a future event, (the likelihood of its coming to be) was of the order of tomorrow’s sunrise (i.e., a certainty), and not of the order of rainfall on the morrow (which would be contingent).<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">106. The distinction between conclusions that are necessary and those that are contingent is an important part of the study of logic, at the basis of the laws of the syllogism. For further discussion of this aspect of Kaspi’s exegesis, see above pp. 44–46.</i> This promise of the land that was made to Abraham was more secure and well bound than any of the earlier promises made to him in this regard, for it was by way of a covenant.<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">107. Here Kaspi attempts to show that what appears to be mere repetition of an earlier promise, and therefore a redundancy, is in fact quite necessary. The question of Scriptural redundancy is discussed again on fol. 152 ff.</i> (This is shown) in truth by the fact that when Abraham asked of the Lord, “Whereby shall I know that I shall inherit it?”, which the wise know was because one can only rely on that which is proved, the Lord’s answer was essentially to establish a covenant. The reason for this was that future events from the Lord of a prophetic nature (are proven by a covenant), just as things that exist at present are proven by a wise teacher who adduces conclusion from necessary and independent premises. It is known that the Lord interchanges modes, i.e., the prophetic modes and those of the rational sciences. Consequently Abraham inquired correctly when he said, “Whereby shall I know that I shall inherit it?” and the Lord answered him correctly. This was similar to if Averroës had asked Aristotle, “How shall I know that every man is sentient?” to which the reply would have been, “Know that every man is an animal, and every animal is sentient.”<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">108. This is the type of syllogism of a categorical nature that is analyzed by Aristotle in the <i>Prior Analytics</i> I, 4, 25b. The same syllogism is found in <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i> (MK II 154). On the use of this principle in Kaspi’s exegesis, see above pp. 44–46.</i> This was how Abraham, in an imprecise manner, inquired of the Lord, in accordance with the dimension of the prophetic mode, which is appropriate for future events. The Lord answered him with the appropriate means by making a necessary proof consisting of a covenant that verified that the future decree was necessary, not contingent. The Lord, however, preceded that proof by stating, “Take me a heifer …” and that entire passage, by way of introducing the desired statement, (to answer) the request to know that the matter was as necessary as tomorrow’s sunrise.
Accordingly, Abraham’s words “whereby shall I know that I shall inherit it” were as if he had said, “Lord God, | on several occasions you have promised me that ‘this land will I give unto you,’ but I do not know if this future decree is necessary or contingent, and therefore I am in doubt and afraid.<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">109. Elsewhere (MK II 289, AK II 51) Kaspi explains that a prophet might be uncertain of the meaning of a divine statement, for a prophet has only the word-characters (<i>mahut ha-millot</i>) to go by. Here God’s words do not indicate whether the promise is necessary or contingent, so Abraham is unsure.</i> Explain this to me.” The Lord then fulfilled Abraham’s desire through the making of the covenant, which informed Abraham that (the promise) was a necessary one. It was for this reason that Abraham said “how shall I know” and did not say “how shall I believe” or “how shall I think” or “how is it possible,” as had been said earlier in regard to the two earlier promises, i.e., the promise of seed, and that of———.<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">110. Here the MS is unclear, with the word <i>devaro</i> inserted.</i> In this regard, Maimonides explained <i>’emunah</i> (“faith”) and <i>ha’amanah</i> (“the act of believing”) in the <i>Guide</i> I:50. In the <i>Posterior Analytics</i> it has been explained that what is not known by means of a necessary proof is considered a “justifiable conception,” or a thought that is right and just.<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">111. In the <i>Posterior Analytics</i> I, 33, 88b, Aristotle says: “opinion may be concerned with that which is true or false, and can be otherwise: opinion is in fact the grasp of a premise which is immediate but not necessary … (on the other hand scientific knowledge) is universal, and proceeds by necessary connections, and that which is necessary cannot be otherwise.”</i> It is similar with the mode of prophecy, for that which is not known with certainty, as the knowledge of the future rising of the sun, is described in these terms (i.e., “belief”). Therefore earlier it states, “And He counted it to him for righteousness” (i.e., Abraham’s acceptance of the promise of seed), for God had not informed Abraham that it was a necessary promise, and Abraham did not ask for such (assurance) except in this case (of the land), for whatever reason. It was only after Abraham did well in the binding of Isaac that the Lord, of His own accord, provided Abraham the necessary knowledge, i.e., the (necessary) great multiplication of his seed, in the words “by myself have I sworn.”<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">112. Gen. 22:16.</i> Had the Lord made (such) a covenant with Jacob, or sworn on the words “I will surely do thee good and make thy seed as the sand of the sea, which cannot be numbered for multitude,”<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">113. Gen. 32:13.</i> then Jacob would not have feared on account of Esau.<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">114. Gen. 32:8.</i> (His fear) was not because Jacob was foolish, he merely followed Abraham’s thinking (in not asking for such a covenant regarding seed), for Abraham did not ask “how shall I know …” in relation to the promise of his personal benefits, or of the multiplication of his seed. Those things were close in time to being fulfilled, as well as close in reality, for they were naturally probable. (This is) enough on this subject.
In the <i>Kesef Sigim</i> I asked what was the sin of Israel for which God decreed such troubles on them, | and I also said that in any case the writer of the Torah should not have mentioned their sufferings without (also mentioning) their sins.<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">115. Apparently the question was one of theodicy: How could this affliction in a strange land be deserved, if those people were not yet born?</i> For such (undeserved punishment) is considered evil and distasteful by the masses, and even by those few people (close to) the Lord. The answer is that the Lord did not intend this (decree) by way of suffering and punishment, neither did He intend to burden Abraham’s heart, for His intention throughout this vision was to impart good news to him. If one would ask, how can “and they shall serve them and they shall afflict them” be good news, the answer is to be found by asking, how could God’s promise to Manoah’s wife, stating “behold now, thou art barren … but thou shalt conceive and bear a son,”<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">116. Judg. 13:3.</i> be good news? After all (giving birth) involves the problems of suffering, pain, and fear, in accordance with the characterization of giving birth as “in pain thou shall bring forth children,”<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">117. Gen. 4:16.</i> i.e., conception and giving birth are compared to suffering. It is similar in the precise statement “as soon as Zion travailed, she brought forth her children,”<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">118. Isa. 66:8.</i> where the term <i>ḥalah</i> is not related to <i>ḥayil</i> (“strength”). (In the case of Abraham) the tidings of a child and children are mentioned in a precise way in that they only mention the existence of the child (and not the suffering that precedes), when it says, “I will bless her and moreover I will give thee a son of her,”<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">119. Gen. 17:6.</i> and elsewhere, “I will certainly return unto thee when the season cometh around, and lo, thy wife shall have a son.”<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">120. Gen. 18:10.</i> This was as if to inform us that (God) did not wish her (to have) the pains preceding the birth of the child, for her benefit and satisfaction——— ———.<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">121. At this point the MS has two words <i>sekhel veṭov</i> (“intelligent and good”) whose reference is unclear, for they could refer to God, to Sarah, or the child.</i> Yet it was impossible to achieve such a benefit without its preceding pains and suffering.<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">122. At one point in Kaspi’s comments on Job (AS I 146), he says that the three friends of Job share the opinion that every righteous man properly experiences hardship, so that ultimately his happiness is increased.</i>
Reliable evidence for this is provided by those going to sea in ships who risk and endanger their lives in order to profit thousands of gold pieces when they reach their destination, and then they thank God for His mercies, as David has written.<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">123. Ps. 18:17–18, which repeats David’s praise in II Sam. 22:17: <br>He sent from on high, He took me; <br>He drew me out of many waters. <br>He delivered me from my enemy most strong.</i> Should they, on account of the dangers, avoid traveling on the sea or not rejoice at the profits? It is similar with the merchants traveling in the desert, and (the soldiers) who conquer cities in war, and in so doing suffer many things, including death. Why should I need to mention these examples, when the achievement of all benefits in the world is just like this. Even women and children know that a man achieves nothing by merely lying with his wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">124. In <i>Ḥaṣoṣrot Kesef</i> (As I 59) Kaspi similarly rebukes the masses, who “think that intercourse is in itself good and proper, and who like cockerels are constantly with their wives.” On the other hand, however, in <i>Maṣref La-Kesef</i> (MK II 256) Kaspi says: “they have said, ‘the greater the man the greater his evil inclination’ [BT <i>Succah</i> 52a]; consequently Moses’ natural functions did not weaken even when he was eighty years old … so one should not think that Moses withdrew from women, for he was no Augustinian or Carmelite (monk).”</i>
Now this is especially true here (in the case of Abraham’s descendants in Egypt), when those problems mentioned, i.e., the servitude and affliction, are themselves the cause of “and afterwards they shall come out with great substance,” and “in the fourth generation they shall come back hither.” This is true for several reasons: Firstly, were it not | for (these problems), there would not have occurred “the children of Israel sighed … and their cry came up to God,”<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">125. Exod. 2:23.</i> which was the essential reason that the Lord took them from Egypt. This is also true of “I have surely seen the affliction of my people which are in Egypt, and I have heard their cry by reason of their taskmasters.”<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">126. Exod. 3:7.</i> Had the children of Israel lived in Egypt in peace, and had none of this (affliction) existed, they would not have inherited the land of Canaan or built the Temple. The same applies (to all the benefits) listed in the <i>Hallel</i> in “how many degrees of goodness has God wrought with us,”<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">127. This refers to the Passover Haggadah, which quotes <i>Sifre’ Deut</i>., chap. 337.</i> which does not include “every woman shall ask of her neighbor and of her that sojourneth in her house,”<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">128. Exod. 3:22.</i> and “And they asked of the Egyptians jewels of silver, and jewels of gold … and raiment … and they despoiled the Egyptians.”<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">129. Exod. 12:35–36.</i> Altogether, the servitude and affliction were the essential cause of the success of our forefathers in improving body and soul, the ultimate (goal).<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">130. This paraphrases the <i>Guide</i> III:27, which states: “the law as a whole aims at two things: the welfare of the soul and the welfare of the body.” Kaspi expands upon this in <i>Sefer Ha-Musar</i> (AS II 61, <i>Ethical Wills</i> p. 133).</i> This is to be compared to those at sea en route to their destination, who are led to another destination against their will by a storm brought by the Lord, only to profit many times more (than originally planned), or to profit instead of losing their lives and money had they reached their intended destination. This is a constant occurrence on land and sea. The same is true of our people (in Egypt) in their success (in improving their body and soul). The enslavement of Joseph after he was sold and imprisoned and tied (hand) and foot was similar. The whole matter is to be compared to a sick man who is fed “sangvin”<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">131. In the Parma MS of <i>Ḳevuṣat Kesef</i>, Kaspi refers to this potion as a medicine compounded of honey and vinegar. References to this medicine are found in Averroës’ <i>Epitome of Aristotle’s Physics</i> (Riva di Trento, 1559) and Maimonides’ <i>Be’ur Ha-Shemot Ha-Refu’ot</i>, ed. S. Muntner (Jerusalem: Mosad Harav Kook, 1969), p. 114, where in a responsum it is advocated to take a solution of vinegar and honey, about an hour after a meal, to assist the digestive process. The editor translates the term as “oxymel.”</i> (which is a bitter potion) causing him to recuperate and stay alive, only to die later when he is fed honey.<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">132. The point here is that the bitter vinegar is an active agent that effects the recovery.</i> The sages compare this to one whose organ was severed in wartime by an enemy, resulting in a loss of blood that saved his life, for (all along) he had been in need of bloodletting to prevent his death.<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">133. Bloodletting is discussed in BT <i>Shabbat</i> 129a. In Maimonides’ <i>Be’ur Ha-Shemot Ha-Refu’ot</i>, p. 143, he states: “[bloodletting] cleanses the blood and adjusts the liver, to facilitate the production of healthier blood.”</i> Why should I continue at length in a matter which even babes know?<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">134. The idea of suffering as a discipline and as a didactic tool has a long history in Biblical thought. The “suffering servant of the Lord” is first found in Isaiah, and also is found in the Book of Job, in the speeches of Eliphaz and Elihu (Job 5:17, 33:16–30, 36:9–12).
